**[ASSISTANT]**

1	---
     2	name: file-reading
     3	description: "Use this skill when a file has been uploaded but its content is NOT in your context — only its path at /mnt/user-data/uploads/ is listed in an uploaded_files block. This skill is a router: it tells you which tool to use for each file type (pdf, docx, xlsx, csv, json, images, archives, ebooks) so you read the right amount the right way instead of blindly running cat on a binary. Triggers: any mention of /mnt/user-data/uploads/, an uploaded_files section, a file_path tag, or a user asking about an uploaded file you have not yet read. Do NOT use this skill if the file content is already visible in your context inside a documents block — you already have it."
     4	compatibility: "claude.ai, Claude Desktop, Cowork — any surface where uploads land at /mnt/user-data/uploads/"
     5	license: Proprietary. LICENSE.txt has complete terms
     6	---
     7	
     8	# Reading Uploaded Files
     9	
    10	## Why this skill exists
    11	
    12	When a user uploads a file in claude.ai, Claude Desktop, or Cowork,
    13	the file is written to `/mnt/user-data/uploads/<filename>` and you are told the path
    14	in an `<uploaded_files>` block. **The content is not in your context.**
    15	You must go read it.
    16	
    17	The naive thing — `cat /mnt/user-data/uploads/whatever` — is wrong for
    18	most files:
    19	
    20	- On a PDF it prints binary garbage.
    21	- On a 100MB CSV it floods your context with rows you will never use.
    22	- On a DOCX it prints the raw ZIP bytes.
    23	- On an image it does nothing useful at all.
    24	
    25	This skill tells you the right first move for each type, and when to
    26	hand off to a deeper skill.
    27	
    28	## General protocol
    29	
    30	1. **Look at the extension.** That is your dispatch key.
    31	2. **Stat before you read.** Large files need sampling, not slurping.
    32	   ```bash
    33	   stat -c '%s bytes, %y' /mnt/user-data/uploads/report.pdf
    34	   file /mnt/user-data/uploads/report.pdf
    35	   ```
    36	3. **Read just enough to answer the user's question.** If they asked
    37	   "how many rows are in this CSV", don't load the whole thing into
    38	   pandas — `wc -l` gives a fast approximation (it counts newlines,
    39	   not CSV records, so it may over-count if quoted fields contain
    40	   embedded newlines).
    41	4. **If a dedicated skill exists, go read it.** The table below tells
    42	   you when. The dedicated skills cover editing, creating, and advanced
    43	   operations that this skill does not.
    44	
    45	## `extract-text`
    46	
    47	For docx, odt, epub, xlsx, pptx, rtf, and ipynb the first move is
    48	`extract-text <file>`. It emits markdown for docx/odt/epub (headings,
    49	bold, lists, links, tables), tab-separated rows under `## Sheet:`
    50	headers for xlsx, text under `## Slide N` headers for pptx, fenced
    51	code cells for ipynb, and plain text for rtf. Pass `--format <fmt>`
    52	when the extension is wrong or absent (e.g., `--format xlsx` on an
    53	`.xlsm`). If it errors on a file, `pandoc <file> -t plain` is a
    54	fallback; for xlsx/pptx, fall back to the dedicated skill's
    55	Python-based approach (openpyxl / python-pptx).
    56	
    57	## Dispatch table
    58	
    59	| Extension                         | First move                                           | Dedicated skill                           |
    60	| --------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------- |
    61	| `.pdf`                            | Content inventory (see PDF section)                  | `/mnt/skills/public/pdf-reading/SKILL.md` |
    62	| `.docx`                           | `extract-text`                                       | `/mnt/skills/public/docx/SKILL.md`        |
    63	| `.doc` (legacy)                   | Convert to `.docx` first                             | `/mnt/skills/public/docx/SKILL.md`        |
    64	| `.xlsx`                           | `extract-text`                                       | `/mnt/skills/public/xlsx/SKILL.md`        |
    65	| `.xlsm`                           | `extract-text --format xlsx`                         | `/mnt/skills/public/xlsx/SKILL.md`        |
    66	| `.xls` (legacy)                   | `pd.read_excel(engine="xlrd")` — openpyxl rejects it | `/mnt/skills/public/xlsx/SKILL.md`        |
    67	| `.ods`                            | `pd.read_excel(engine="odf")` — openpyxl rejects it  | `/mnt/skills/public/xlsx/SKILL.md`        |
    68	| `.pptx`                           | `extract-text`                                       | `/mnt/skills/public/pptx/SKILL.md`        |
    69	| `.ppt` (legacy)                   | Convert to `.pptx` first                             | `/mnt/skills/public/pptx/SKILL.md`        |
    70	| `.csv`, `.tsv`                    | `pandas` with `nrows`                                | — (below)                                 |
    71	| `.json`, `.jsonl`                 | `jq` for structure                                   | — (below)                                 |
    72	| `.jpg`, `.png`, `.gif`, `.webp`   | Already in your context as vision input              | — (below)                                 |
    73	| `.zip`, `.tar`, `.tar.gz`         | List contents, do **not** auto-extract               | — (below)                                 |
    74	| `.gz` (single file)               | `zcat \| head` — no manifest to list                 | — (below)                                 |
    75	| `.epub`, `.odt`                   | `extract-text`                                       | — (below)                                 |
    76	| `.rtf`                            | `extract-text`                                       | — (below)                                 |
    77	| `.ipynb`                          | `extract-text`                                       | — (below)                                 |
    78	| `.txt`, `.md`, `.log`, code files | `wc -c` then `head` or full `cat`                    | — (below)                                 |
    79	| Unknown                           | `file` then decide                                   | —                                         |
    80	
    81	---
    82	
    83	## PDF
    84	
    85	**Never** `cat` a PDF — it prints binary garbage.
    86	
    87	Quick first move — get the page count and check if text is extractable:
    88	
    89	```bash
    90	pdfinfo /mnt/user-data/uploads/report.pdf
    91	pdftotext -f 1 -l 1 /mnt/user-data/uploads/report.pdf - | head -20
    92	```
    93	
    94	Then peek at the text content:
    95	
    96	```python
    97	from pypdf import PdfReader
    98	r = PdfReader("/mnt/user-data/uploads/report.pdf")
    99	print(f"{len(r.pages)} pages")
   100	print(r.pages[0].extract_text()[:2000])
   101	```
   102	
   103	For anything beyond a quick peek — figures, tables, attachments,
   104	forms, scanned PDFs, visual inspection, or choosing a reading strategy
   105	— go read `/mnt/skills/public/pdf-reading/SKILL.md`. It covers
   106	content inventory, text extraction vs. page rasterization, embedded
   107	content extraction, and document-type-aware reading strategies.
   108	
   109	For PDF form filling, creation, merging, splitting, or watermarking,
   110	go read `/mnt/skills/public/pdf/SKILL.md`.
   111	
   112	---
   113	
   114	## DOCX / DOC
   115	
   116	The `docx` skill covers editing, creating, tracked changes, images.
   117	Read it if you need any of those. For a quick look:
   118	
   119	```bash
   120	extract-text /mnt/user-data/uploads/memo.docx | head -200
   121	```
   122	
   123	Legacy `.doc` (not `.docx`) must be converted first — see the `docx`
   124	skill.
   125	
   126	---
   127	
   128	## XLSX / XLS / spreadsheets
   129	
   130	The `xlsx` skill covers formulas, formatting, charts, creating. Read
   131	it if you need any of those. For a quick look at an `.xlsx`:
   132	
   133	```bash
   134	extract-text /mnt/user-data/uploads/data.xlsx | head -100
   135	```
   136	
   137	For `.xlsm`, add `--format xlsx` (same zip structure; only the
   138	extension differs). When you need a structured preview in Python:
   139	
   140	```python
   141	from openpyxl import load_workbook
   142	wb = load_workbook("/mnt/user-data/uploads/data.xlsx", read_only=True)
   143	print("Sheets:", wb.sheetnames)
   144	ws = wb.active
   145	for row in ws.iter_rows(max_row=5, values_only=True):
   146	    print(row)
   147	```
   148	
   149	`read_only=True` matters — without it, openpyxl loads the entire
   150	workbook into memory, which breaks on large files. Do not trust
   151	`ws.max_row` in read-only mode: many non-Excel writers omit the
   152	dimension record, so it comes back `None` or wrong. If you need a row
   153	count, iterate or use pandas.
   154	
   155	**Legacy `.xls`** — openpyxl raises `InvalidFileException`. Use:
   156	
   157	```python
   158	import pandas as pd
   159	df = pd.read_excel("/mnt/user-data/uploads/old.xls", engine="xlrd", nrows=5)
   160	```
   161	
   162	**`.ods` (OpenDocument)** — openpyxl also rejects this. Use:
   163	
   164	```python
   165	import pandas as pd
   166	df = pd.read_excel("/mnt/user-data/uploads/data.ods", engine="odf", nrows=5)
   167	```
   168	
   169	---
   170	
   171	## PPTX
   172	
   173	```bash
   174	extract-text /mnt/user-data/uploads/deck.pptx | head -200
   175	```
   176	
   177	**Legacy `.ppt`** — convert to `.pptx` first via LibreOffice; see
   178	`/mnt/skills/public/pptx/SKILL.md` for the sandbox-safe
   179	`scripts/office/soffice.py` wrapper (bare `soffice` hangs here because
   180	the seccomp filter blocks the `AF_UNIX` sockets LibreOffice uses for
   181	instance management).
   182	
   183	For anything beyond reading, go to `/mnt/skills/public/pptx/SKILL.md`.
   184	
   185	---
   186	
   187	## CSV / TSV
   188	
   189	**Do not** `cat` or `head` these blindly. A CSV with a 50KB quoted cell
   190	in row 1 will wreck your `head -5`. Use pandas with `nrows`:
   191	
   192	```python
   193	import pandas as pd
   194	df = pd.read_csv("/mnt/user-data/uploads/data.csv", nrows=5)
   195	print(df)
   196	print()
   197	print(df.dtypes)
   198	```
   199	
   200	Approximate row count without loading (over-counts if the file has
   201	RFC-4180 quoted newlines — the same quoted-cell case this section
   202	warned about above):
   203	
   204	```bash
   205	wc -l /mnt/user-data/uploads/data.csv
   206	```
   207	
   208	Full analysis only after you know the shape:
   209	
   210	```python
   211	df = pd.read_csv("/mnt/user-data/uploads/data.csv")
   212	print(df.describe())
   213	```
   214	
   215	TSV: same, with `sep="\t"`.
   216	
   217	---
   218	
   219	## JSON / JSONL
   220	
   221	Structure first, content second:
   222	
   223	```bash
   224	jq 'type' /mnt/user-data/uploads/data.json
   225	jq 'if type == "array" then length elif type == "object" then keys else . end' /mnt/user-data/uploads/data.json
   226	```
   227	
   228	(`keys` errors on scalar JSON roots — a bare `"hello"` or `42` is valid
   229	JSON per RFC 7159 — so guard the branch.)
   230	
   231	Then drill into what the user actually asked about.
   232	
   233	JSONL (one object per line) — do **not** `jq` the whole file; work line
   234	by line:
   235	
   236	```bash
   237	head -3 /mnt/user-data/uploads/data.jsonl | jq .
   238	wc -l /mnt/user-data/uploads/data.jsonl
   239	```
   240	
   241	---
   242	
   243	## Images (JPG / PNG / GIF / WEBP)
   244	
   245	**You can already see uploaded images.** They are injected into your
   246	context as vision inputs alongside the `<uploaded_files>` pointer. You
   247	do not need to read them from disk to describe them.
   248	
   249	The disk copy is only needed if you are going to **process** the image
   250	programmatically:
   251	
   252	```python
   253	from PIL import Image
   254	img = Image.open("/mnt/user-data/uploads/photo.jpg")
   255	print(img.size, img.mode, img.format)
   256	```
   257	
   258	For OCR on an image (text extraction, not description):
   259	
   260	```python
   261	import pytesseract
   262	print(pytesseract.image_to_string(img))
   263	```
   264	
   265	Note: the client resizes images larger than 2000×2000 down to that
   266	bound and re-encodes as JPEG before upload, so the disk copy may not
   267	be the user's original bytes. For most processing this doesn't matter;
   268	if the user is asking about original-resolution pixel data, flag it.
   269	
   270	---
   271	
   272	## Archives (ZIP / TAR / TAR.GZ)
   273	
   274	**List first. Extract never — unless the user explicitly asks.**
   275	Archives can be huge, contain path traversal, or nest forever.
   276	
   277	```bash
   278	unzip -l /mnt/user-data/uploads/bundle.zip
   279	tar -tf /mnt/user-data/uploads/bundle.tar
   280	```
   281	
   282	GNU tar auto-detects compression — `tar -tf` works on `.tar`,
   283	`.tar.gz`, `.tar.bz2`, `.tar.xz` alike. Don't hard-code `-z`.
   284	
   285	If the user wants one file from inside, extract just that one:
   286	
   287	```bash
   288	unzip -p /mnt/user-data/uploads/bundle.zip path/inside/file.txt
   289	```
   290	
   291	**Standalone `.gz`** (not a tar) compresses a single file — there is
   292	no manifest to list. Just peek at the decompressed content:
   293	
   294	```bash
   295	zcat /mnt/user-data/uploads/data.json.gz | head -50
   296	```
   297	
   298	---
   299	
   300	## EPUB / ODT
   301	
   302	```bash
   303	extract-text /mnt/user-data/uploads/book.epub | head -200
   304	```
   305	
   306	For long ebooks, pipe through `head` — you rarely need the whole thing
   307	to answer a question.
   308	
   309	---
   310	
   311	## RTF / IPYNB
   312	
   313	```bash
   314	extract-text /mnt/user-data/uploads/notes.rtf | head -200
   315	extract-text /mnt/user-data/uploads/notebook.ipynb | head -200
   316	```
   317	
   318	---
   319	
   320	## Plain text / code / logs
   321	
   322	Check the size first:
   323	
   324	```bash
   325	wc -c /mnt/user-data/uploads/app.log
   326	```
   327	
   328	- **Under ~20KB**: `cat` is fine.
   329	- **Over ~20KB**: `head -100` and `tail -100` to orient. If the user
   330	  asked about something specific, `grep` for it. Load the whole thing
   331	  only if you genuinely need all of it.
   332	
   333	For log files, the user almost always cares about the end:
   334	
   335	```bash
   336	tail -200 /mnt/user-data/uploads/app.log
   337	```
   338	
   339	---
   340	
   341	## Unknown extension
   342	
   343	```bash
   344	file /mnt/user-data/uploads/mystery.bin
   345	xxd /mnt/user-data/uploads/mystery.bin | head -5
   346	```
   347	
   348	`file` identifies most things. `xxd` head shows magic bytes. If `file`
   349	says "data" and the hex doesn't match anything you recognize, ask the
   350	user what it is instead of guessing.

**[ASSISTANT]**

Let me read the document first.     1	---
     2	name: file-reading
     3	description: "Use this skill when a file has been uploaded but its content is NOT in your context — only its path at /mnt/user-data/uploads/ is listed in an uploaded_files block. This skill is a router: it tells you which tool to use for each file type (pdf, docx, xlsx, csv, json, images, archives, ebooks) so you read the right amount the right way instead of blindly running cat on a binary. Triggers: any mention of /mnt/user-data/uploads/, an uploaded_files section, a file_path tag, or a user asking about an uploaded file you have not yet read. Do NOT use this skill if the file content is already visible in your context inside a documents block — you already have it."
     4	compatibility: "claude.ai, Claude Desktop, Cowork — any surface where uploads land at /mnt/user-data/uploads/"
     5	license: Proprietary. LICENSE.txt has complete terms
     6	---
     7	
     8	# Reading Uploaded Files
     9	
    10	## Why this skill exists
    11	
    12	When a user uploads a file in claude.ai, Claude Desktop, or Cowork,
    13	the file is written to `/mnt/user-data/uploads/<filename>` and you are told the path
    14	in an `<uploaded_files>` block. **The content is not in your context.**
    15	You must go read it.
    16	
    17	The naive thing — `cat /mnt/user-data/uploads/whatever` — is wrong for
    18	most files:
    19	
    20	- On a PDF it prints binary garbage.
    21	- On a 100MB CSV it floods your context with rows you will never use.
    22	- On a DOCX it prints the raw ZIP bytes.
    23	- On an image it does nothing useful at all.
    24	
    25	This skill tells you the right first move for each type, and when to
    26	hand off to a deeper skill.
    27	
    28	## General protocol
    29	
    30	1. **Look at the extension.** That is your dispatch key.
    31	2. **Stat before you read.** Large files need sampling, not slurping.
    32	   ```bash
    33	   stat -c '%s bytes, %y' /mnt/user-data/uploads/report.pdf
    34	   file /mnt/user-data/uploads/report.pdf
    35	   ```
    36	3. **Read just enough to answer the user's question.** If they asked
    37	   "how many rows are in this CSV", don't load the whole thing into
    38	   pandas — `wc -l` gives a fast approximation (it counts newlines,
    39	   not CSV records, so it may over-count if quoted fields contain
    40	   embedded newlines).
    41	4. **If a dedicated skill exists, go read it.** The table below tells
    42	   you when. The dedicated skills cover editing, creating, and advanced
    43	   operations that this skill does not.
    44	
    45	## `extract-text`
    46	
    47	For docx, odt, epub, xlsx, pptx, rtf, and ipynb the first move is
    48	`extract-text <file>`. It emits markdown for docx/odt/epub (headings,
    49	bold, lists, links, tables), tab-separated rows under `## Sheet:`
    50	headers for xlsx, text under `## Slide N` headers for pptx, fenced
    51	code cells for ipynb, and plain text for rtf. Pass `--format <fmt>`
    52	when the extension is wrong or absent (e.g., `--format xlsx` on an
    53	`.xlsm`). If it errors on a file, `pandoc <file> -t plain` is a
    54	fallback; for xlsx/pptx, fall back to the dedicated skill's
    55	Python-based approach (openpyxl / python-pptx).
    56	
    57	## Dispatch table
    58	
    59	| Extension                         | First move                                           | Dedicated skill                           |
    60	| --------------------------------- | ---------------------------------------------------- | ----------------------------------------- |
    61	| `.pdf`                            | Content inventory (see PDF section)                  | `/mnt/skills/public/pdf-reading/SKILL.md` |
    62	| `.docx`                           | `extract-text`                                       | `/mnt/skills/public/docx/SKILL.md`        |
    63	| `.doc` (legacy)                   | Convert to `.docx` first                             | `/mnt/skills/public/docx/SKILL.md`        |
    64	| `.xlsx`                           | `extract-text`                                       | `/mnt/skills/public/xlsx/SKILL.md`        |
    65	| `.xlsm`                           | `extract-text --format xlsx`                         | `/mnt/skills/public/xlsx/SKILL.md`        |
    66	| `.xls` (legacy)                   | `pd.read_excel(engine="xlrd")` — openpyxl rejects it | `/mnt/skills/public/xlsx/SKILL.md`        |
    67	| `.ods`                            | `pd.read_excel(engine="odf")` — openpyxl rejects it  | `/mnt/skills/public/xlsx/SKILL.md`        |
    68	| `.pptx`                           | `extract-text`                                       | `/mnt/skills/public/pptx/SKILL.md`        |
    69	| `.ppt` (legacy)                   | Convert to `.pptx` first                             | `/mnt/skills/public/pptx/SKILL.md`        |
    70	| `.csv`, `.tsv`                    | `pandas` with `nrows`                                | — (below)                                 |
    71	| `.json`, `.jsonl`                 | `jq` for structure                                   | — (below)                                 |
    72	| `.jpg`, `.png`, `.gif`, `.webp`   | Already in your context as vision input              | — (below)                                 |
    73	| `.zip`, `.tar`, `.tar.gz`         | List contents, do **not** auto-extract               | — (below)                                 |
    74	| `.gz` (single file)               | `zcat \| head` — no manifest to list                 | — (below)                                 |
    75	| `.epub`, `.odt`                   | `extract-text`                                       | — (below)                                 |
    76	| `.rtf`                            | `extract-text`                                       | — (below)                                 |
    77	| `.ipynb`                          | `extract-text`                                       | — (below)                                 |
    78	| `.txt`, `.md`, `.log`, code files | `wc -c` then `head` or full `cat`                    | — (below)                                 |
    79	| Unknown                           | `file` then decide                                   | —                                         |
    80	
    81	---
    82	
    83	## PDF
    84	
    85	**Never** `cat` a PDF — it prints binary garbage.
    86	
    87	Quick first move — get the page count and check if text is extractable:
    88	
    89	```bash
    90	pdfinfo /mnt/user-data/uploads/report.pdf
    91	pdftotext -f 1 -l 1 /mnt/user-data/uploads/report.pdf - | head -20
    92	```
    93	
    94	Then peek at the text content:
    95	
    96	```python
    97	from pypdf import PdfReader
    98	r = PdfReader("/mnt/user-data/uploads/report.pdf")
    99	print(f"{len(r.pages)} pages")
   100	print(r.pages[0].extract_text()[:2000])
   101	```
   102	
   103	For anything beyond a quick peek — figures, tables, attachments,
   104	forms, scanned PDFs, visual inspection, or choosing a reading strategy
   105	— go read `/mnt/skills/public/pdf-reading/SKILL.md`. It covers
   106	content inventory, text extraction vs. page rasterization, embedded
   107	content extraction, and document-type-aware reading strategies.
   108	
   109	For PDF form filling, creation, merging, splitting, or watermarking,
   110	go read `/mnt/skills/public/pdf/SKILL.md`.
   111	
   112	---
   113	
   114	## DOCX / DOC
   115	
   116	The `docx` skill covers editing, creating, tracked changes, images.
   117	Read it if you need any of those. For a quick look:
   118	
   119	```bash
   120	extract-text /mnt/user-data/uploads/memo.docx | head -200
   121	```
   122	
   123	Legacy `.doc` (not `.docx`) must be converted first — see the `docx`
   124	skill.
   125	
   126	---
   127	
   128	## XLSX / XLS / spreadsheets
   129	
   130	The `xlsx` skill covers formulas, formatting, charts, creating. Read
   131	it if you need any of those. For a quick look at an `.xlsx`:
   132	
   133	```bash
   134	extract-text /mnt/user-data/uploads/data.xlsx | head -100
   135	```
   136	
   137	For `.xlsm`, add `--format xlsx` (same zip structure; only the
   138	extension differs). When you need a structured preview in Python:
   139	
   140	```python
   141	from openpyxl import load_workbook
   142	wb = load_workbook("/mnt/user-data/uploads/data.xlsx", read_only=True)
   143	print("Sheets:", wb.sheetnames)
   144	ws = wb.active
   145	for row in ws.iter_rows(max_row=5, values_only=True):
   146	    print(row)
   147	```
   148	
   149	`read_only=True` matters — without it, openpyxl loads the entire
   150	workbook into memory, which breaks on large files. Do not trust
   151	`ws.max_row` in read-only mode: many non-Excel writers omit the
   152	dimension record, so it comes back `None` or wrong. If you need a row
   153	count, iterate or use pandas.
   154	
   155	**Legacy `.xls`** — openpyxl raises `InvalidFileException`. Use:
   156	
   157	```python
   158	import pandas as pd
   159	df = pd.read_excel("/mnt/user-data/uploads/old.xls", engine="xlrd", nrows=5)
   160	```
   161	
   162	**`.ods` (OpenDocument)** — openpyxl also rejects this. Use:
   163	
   164	```python
   165	import pandas as pd
   166	df = pd.read_excel("/mnt/user-data/uploads/data.ods", engine="odf", nrows=5)
   167	```
   168	
   169	---
   170	
   171	## PPTX
   172	
   173	```bash
   174	extract-text /mnt/user-data/uploads/deck.pptx | head -200
   175	```
   176	
   177	**Legacy `.ppt`** — convert to `.pptx` first via LibreOffice; see
   178	`/mnt/skills/public/pptx/SKILL.md` for the sandbox-safe
   179	`scripts/office/soffice.py` wrapper (bare `soffice` hangs here because
   180	the seccomp filter blocks the `AF_UNIX` sockets LibreOffice uses for
   181	instance management).
   182	
   183	For anything beyond reading, go to `/mnt/skills/public/pptx/SKILL.md`.
   184	
   185	---
   186	
   187	## CSV / TSV
   188	
   189	**Do not** `cat` or `head` these blindly. A CSV with a 50KB quoted cell
   190	in row 1 will wreck your `head -5`. Use pandas with `nrows`:
   191	
   192	```python
   193	import pandas as pd
   194	df = pd.read_csv("/mnt/user-data/uploads/data.csv", nrows=5)
   195	print(df)
   196	print()
   197	print(df.dtypes)
   198	```
   199	
   200	Approximate row count without loading (over-counts if the file has
   201	RFC-4180 quoted newlines — the same quoted-cell case this section
   202	warned about above):
   203	
   204	```bash
   205	wc -l /mnt/user-data/uploads/data.csv
   206	```
   207	
   208	Full analysis only after you know the shape:
   209	
   210	```python
   211	df = pd.read_csv("/mnt/user-data/uploads/data.csv")
   212	print(df.describe())
   213	```
   214	
   215	TSV: same, with `sep="\t"`.
   216	
   217	---
   218	
   219	## JSON / JSONL
   220	
   221	Structure first, content second:
   222	
   223	```bash
   224	jq 'type' /mnt/user-data/uploads/data.json
   225	jq 'if type == "array" then length elif type == "object" then keys else . end' /mnt/user-data/uploads/data.json
   226	```
   227	
   228	(`keys` errors on scalar JSON roots — a bare `"hello"` or `42` is valid
   229	JSON per RFC 7159 — so guard the branch.)
   230	
   231	Then drill into what the user actually asked about.
   232	
   233	JSONL (one object per line) — do **not** `jq` the whole file; work line
   234	by line:
   235	
   236	```bash
   237	head -3 /mnt/user-data/uploads/data.jsonl | jq .
   238	wc -l /mnt/user-data/uploads/data.jsonl
   239	```
   240	
   241	---
   242	
   243	## Images (JPG / PNG / GIF / WEBP)
   244	
   245	**You can already see uploaded images.** They are injected into your
   246	context as vision inputs alongside the `<uploaded_files>` pointer. You
   247	do not need to read them from disk to describe them.
   248	
   249	The disk copy is only needed if you are going to **process** the image
   250	programmatically:
   251	
   252	```python
   253	from PIL import Image
   254	img = Image.open("/mnt/user-data/uploads/photo.jpg")
   255	print(img.size, img.mode, img.format)
   256	```
   257	
   258	For OCR on an image (text extraction, not description):
   259	
   260	```python
   261	import pytesseract
   262	print(pytesseract.image_to_string(img))
   263	```
   264	
   265	Note: the client resizes images larger than 2000×2000 down to that
   266	bound and re-encodes as JPEG before upload, so the disk copy may not
   267	be the user's original bytes. For most processing this doesn't matter;
   268	if the user is asking about original-resolution pixel data, flag it.
   269	
   270	---
   271	
   272	## Archives (ZIP / TAR / TAR.GZ)
   273	
   274	**List first. Extract never — unless the user explicitly asks.**
   275	Archives can be huge, contain path traversal, or nest forever.
   276	
   277	```bash
   278	unzip -l /mnt/user-data/uploads/bundle.zip
   279	tar -tf /mnt/user-data/uploads/bundle.tar
   280	```
   281	
   282	GNU tar auto-detects compression — `tar -tf` works on `.tar`,
   283	`.tar.gz`, `.tar.bz2`, `.tar.xz` alike. Don't hard-code `-z`.
   284	
   285	If the user wants one file from inside, extract just that one:
   286	
   287	```bash
   288	unzip -p /mnt/user-data/uploads/bundle.zip path/inside/file.txt
   289	```
   290	
   291	**Standalone `.gz`** (not a tar) compresses a single file — there is
   292	no manifest to list. Just peek at the decompressed content:
   293	
   294	```bash
   295	zcat /mnt/user-data/uploads/data.json.gz | head -50
   296	```
   297	
   298	---
   299	
   300	## EPUB / ODT
   301	
   302	```bash
   303	extract-text /mnt/user-data/uploads/book.epub | head -200
   304	```
   305	
   306	For long ebooks, pipe through `head` — you rarely need the whole thing
   307	to answer a question.
   308	
   309	---
   310	
   311	## RTF / IPYNB
   312	
   313	```bash
   314	extract-text /mnt/user-data/uploads/notes.rtf | head -200
   315	extract-text /mnt/user-data/uploads/notebook.ipynb | head -200
   316	```
   317	
   318	---
   319	
   320	## Plain text / code / logs
   321	
   322	Check the size first:
   323	
   324	```bash
   325	wc -c /mnt/user-data/uploads/app.log
   326	```
   327	
   328	- **Under ~20KB**: `cat` is fine.
   329	- **Over ~20KB**: `head -100` and `tail -100` to orient. If the user
   330	  asked about something specific, `grep` for it. Load the whole thing
   331	  only if you genuinely need all of it.
   332	
   333	For log files, the user almost always cares about the end:
   334	
   335	```bash
   336	tail -200 /mnt/user-data/uploads/app.log
   337	```
   338	
   339	---
   340	
   341	## Unknown extension
   342	
   343	```bash
   344	file /mnt/user-data/uploads/mystery.bin
   345	xxd /mnt/user-data/uploads/mystery.bin | head -5
   346	```
   347

OSHA says Outreach courses are basic hazard-awareness classes and are not considered certifications or licenses; OSHA cards come from OSHA-authorized trainers after completing 10-hour or 30-hour Outreach classes.\n\nA good disclaimer would be:\n\nThis course is a stagehand orientation and safety-awareness course. It does not replace employer-provided safety training, site-specific training, union training, equipment certification, or an OSHA 10/30 course.\n\nThat is important because OSHA states that employers are responsible for training workers on the specific hazards of their job, and Outreach training does not fulfill training requirements found in OSHA standards.\n\n# **Full Course Plan**\n\n## **Course Length**\n\nFor a marketplace course, I would aim for:\n\n**6 to 8 modules**\n **35 to 50 short lessons**\n **4 to 6 hours of video**\n **Downloadable checklists and quick-reference guides**\n **End-of-course quiz or practical self-assessment**\n\nThis is deep enough to feel valuable but not so long that beginners get overwhelmed.\n\n# **Module 1: Welcome to the Live Event World**\n\n**Purpose:** Give learners the big picture before they walk into a venue.\n\nThis module should explain what stagehands do, where they fit in, and what kinds of calls they may work.\n\n### **Lessons**\n\n- What a Stagehand Actually Does\n\n- The Difference Between Load-In, Show Call, and Load-Out\n\n- Common Work Environments: Arenas, Theaters, Convention Centers, Outdoor Shows\n\n- Who Is on the Crew: Leads, Department Heads, Riggers, Audio, Lighting, Video, Carpenters, Truck Loaders\n\n- What Makes a New Stagehand Valuable\n\n- What This Course Will and Will Not Teach You\n\n### **Key Outcome**\n\nBy the end, learners should understand that being a stagehand is not just “moving boxes.” It is working safely, following instructions, communicating clearly, and supporting a much larger production.\n\n# **Module 2: Safety Mindset Before Skillset**\n\n**Purpose:** Introduce safety as the foundation of being employable.\n\nThis is where your OSHA idea fits, but I would frame it as **jobsite safety awareness for live events**, not “OSHA training.”\n\nOSHA emphasizes that workers have the right to a safe workplace, training in a language they understand, required safety equipment, and the ability to speak up about hazards without retaliation.\n\n### **Lessons**\n\n- Why Safety Comes Before Speed\n\n- Worker Rights and Employer Responsibilities\n\n- The Difference Between Hazard, Risk, and Unsafe Behavior\n\n- Stop Work Mindset: When to Ask Questions or Get a Lead\n\n- Incident Reporting and Near Misses\n\n- Why You Should Never Pretend You Know Something\n\n- How to Listen During a Safety Briefing\n\n### **Practical Activity**\n\nLearner identifies hazards from example jobsite scenarios:\n\n- Open cable path\n\n- Forklift moving nearby\n\n- Stacked road cases\n\n- Wet floor\n\n- Working under rigging\n\n- Poor lighting during load-out\n\n### **Key Outcome**\n\nLearners should understand that safety awareness is part of professionalism, not a separate topic.\n\n# **Module 3: PPE, Clothing, and What to Bring**\n\n**Purpose:** Prepare learners to show up dressed correctly and equipped.\n\nThis is one of the most useful beginner modules because many new people fail before the work even starts because they are not dressed right.\n\nOSHA notes that workers should use required safety gear and receive training on it, and employers are generally responsible for required PPE in many cases.\n\n### **Lessons**\n\n- Show Blacks: What They Are and Why They Matter\n\n- Work Boots, Gloves, Hard Hats, High-Vis, Eye Protection, and Hearing Protection\n\n- What to Bring in Your Work Bag\n\n- What Not to Bring\n\n- Hydration, Food, and Long-Call Preparation\n\n- Personal Readiness: Sleep, Transportation, and Call Time Discipline\n\n- How to Avoid Looking Unprepared on Day One\n\n### **Downloadable**\n\n**New Stagehand Call Checklist**\n\nInclude:\n\n- Black shirt\n\n- Black pants\n\n- Work boots\n\n- Gloves\n\n- Water\n\n- Snacks\n\n- ID\n\n- Phone charger\n\n- Pen/Sharpie\n\n- Small flashlight/headlamp\n\n- Weather layer if outdoor\n\n- Required PPE if assigned\n\n### **Key Outcome**\n\nLearners should know exactly how to show up for their first call.\n\n# **Module 4: Venue and Jobsite Awareness**\n\n**Purpose:** Teach learners how to move through a venue without becoming a hazard.\n\nThis is where many new stagehands struggle. They do not know where to stand, where not to stand, how to stay clear, or how to read the room.\n\n### **Lessons**\n\n- The First 15 Minutes on Site\n\n- How to Find Your Lead or Department\n\n- Stage, Dock, Truck, Ramp, Floor, Backstage, FOH, and Barricade Basics\n\n- Restricted Areas and Why They Matter\n\n- Forklifts, Carts, Pallet Jacks, Pushers, and Moving Equipment\n\n- Overhead Hazards and Working Under Rigging\n\n- Cable Paths, Trip Hazards, and Clean Work Areas\n\n- Emergency Exits, Fire Lanes, and Keeping Paths Clear\n\n### **Practical Activity**\n\nLearner reviews a simple venue map and identifies:\n\n- Safe walking routes\n\n- Truck dock\n\n- Stage\n\n- Emergency exits\n\n- High-traffic zones\n\n- Areas to avoid without direction\n\n### **Key Outcome**\n\nLearners should know how to enter a site, observe, and avoid creating problems.\n\n# **Module 5: Load-In Fundamentals**\n\n**Purpose:** Teach the basic rhythm of a load-in.\n\nThis is one of the core modules. Instead of making “load-in/load-out” one broad module, I would split them. Load-in and load-out feel different, move at different speeds, and have different risks.\n\n### **Lessons**\n\n- What Load-In Means\n\n- Truck Unload Basics\n\n- Road Cases: How They Move, Stack, Lock, and Tip\n\n- Pushing vs. Pulling vs. Guiding Cases\n\n- Ramps, Dock Plates, and Pinch Points\n\n- Building the Room: Stage, Audio, Lighting, Video, Backline, Rigging\n\n- Department Sorting: Getting the Right Case to the Right Place\n\n- Cable Awareness: Feeder, Socapex, Audio Lines, Data, and General Cable Paths\n\n- When to Move Fast and When to Slow Down\n\n- How to Take Direction Without Needing Everything Explained Twice\n\n### **Practical Activity**\n\n“Load-In Flow Exercise”\n Learner puts basic tasks in order:\n\n- Check in\n\n- Safety briefing\n\n- Truck unload\n\n- Sort cases\n\n- Stage build\n\n- Department setup\n\n- Cable runs\n\n- Final cleanup\n\n### **Key Outcome**\n\nLearners should understand the flow of a load-in well enough to be useful, even if they are brand new.\n\n# **Module 6: Stagehand Communication and Crew Etiquette**\n\n**Purpose:** Teach the unspoken rules.\n\nThis may become one of your most valuable modules. New stagehands often fail because of attitude, poor communication, phone use, wandering, or not knowing how to receive direction.\n\n### **Lessons**\n\n- How to Talk to Leads and Department Heads\n\n- The Right Way to Ask Questions\n\n- Radio Basics and Common Callouts\n\n- Hand Signals and Nonverbal Communication\n\n- “Copy,” “Heard,” “Standing By,” and Other Useful Responses\n\n- Phone Etiquette on a Call\n\n- How to Stay Busy Without Freelancing\n\n- How to Handle Correction Without Taking It Personally\n\n- Respecting Touring Crew, Local Crew, and Venue Staff\n\n- What Gets You Asked Back\n\n### **Downloadable**\n\n**Stagehand Communication Cheat Sheet**\n\nInclude phrases like:\n\n- “Who is my lead?”\n\n- “Where do you want this case?”\n\n- “Can you show me the safe way to do that?”\n\n- “Copy.”\n\n- “Standing by.”\n\n- “I have not been trained on that.”\n\n- “Do you want this staged or opened?”\n\n### **Key Outcome**\n\nLearners should know how to behave like someone leads want to keep around.\n\n# **Module 7: Department Basics**\n\n**Purpose:** Give learners a simple overview of the major departments without pretending to fully train them in each one.\n\nThis module helps students understand what is happening around them.\n\n### **Lessons**\n\n- Audio Department Basics\n\n- Lighting Department Basics\n\n- Video Department Basics\n\n- Staging and Carpentry Basics\n\n- Rigging Awareness for Non-Riggers\n\n- Backline and Instruments\n\n- Props, Wardrobe, and Production Support\n\n- Why You Should Not Touch Department Gear Without Direction\n\n- How to Help Without Getting in the Way\n\n### **Important Positioning**\n\nThis should be awareness-level only. For example, do not teach people to rig, operate lifts, run power, or perform specialized work without proper hands-on training and employer authorization.\n\n### **Key Outcome**\n\nLearners should understand the language and basic purpose of each department.\n\n# **Module 8: Tools, Gear, and Handling Basics**\n\n**Purpose:** Teach practical handling skills and basic gear recognition.\n\nThis is a “confidence builder” module. Beginners feel less lost when they can recognize common equipment.\n\n### **Lessons**\n\n- Common Stagehand Tools\n\n- C-Wrenches, Multitools, Flashlights, Tape, and Markers\n\n- Road Case Parts: Lids, Casters, Brakes, Labels, Handles\n\n- Cable Coiling Basics\n\n- Tape Basics: Gaff Tape vs. Electrical Tape vs. Spike Tape\n\n- Pipe, Drape, Decks, Truss, Motors, Cases, and Carts\n\n- Lifting and Team Carry Basics\n\n- Pinch Points, Crush Points, and Stored Energy\n\n- Keeping Gear Organized\n\n### **Practical Activity**\n\nGear ID quiz:\n\n- Road case\n\n- Truss\n\n- Deck\n\n- Cable ramp\n\n- Motor\n\n- Shackles\n\n- Gaff tape\n\n- Feeder cable\n\n- Audio snake\n\n- Barricade\n\n### **Key Outcome**\n\nLearners should recognize common items and understand when they should ask before touching something.\n\n# **Module 9: Load-Out Fundamentals**\n\n**Purpose:** Teach the faster, more chaotic side of the job.\n\nLoad-out needs its own module because it is often where fatigue, speed, and poor communication create problems.\n\n### **Lessons**\n\n- What Makes Load-Out Different From Load-In\n\n- Strike Order: Why Everything Comes Down in a Sequence\n\n- Staying Alert When Everyone Is Tired\n\n- Case Packing and Label Matching\n\n- Cable Strike and Coil Discipline\n\n- Truck Pack Awareness\n\n- Trash, Tape, Sweep, and Final Walkthroughs\n\n- Avoiding “Hurry Up and Get Hurt” Mistakes\n\n- How to Finish Strong\n\n### **Practical Activity**\n\nLearner reviews a load-out scenario and identifies unsafe choices:\n\n- Running with cases\n\n- Pulling cable without checking the other end\n\n- Blocking dock doors\n\n- Standing behind a rolling case on a ramp\n\n- Leaving trash/tape behind\n\n### **Key Outcome**\n\nLearners should understand that load-out is not just “tear it down fast.” It is controlled, sequenced, and safety-focused.\n\n# **Module 10: Getting Hired, Getting Called Back, and Growing**\n\n**Purpose:** Help learners turn training into work.\n\nThis makes the course more valuable than simple jobsite instruction. It gives learners a path forward.\n\n### **Lessons**\n\n- Where Stagehand Work Comes From\n\n- Labor Companies, Venues, Unions, Crewing Apps, and Referrals\n\n- What to Put on a Beginner Resume\n\n- How to Talk About Limited Experience Honestly\n\n- Reliability: The Skill That Gets You Rebooked\n\n- Building a Reputation\n\n- Moving From General Stagehand to Department Specialty\n\n- Next Skills to Learn: OSHA 10, Forklift Awareness, Rigging Awareness, Audio, Lighting, Video, Carpentry\n\n- Your First 30-Day Development Plan\n\n### **Downloadable**\n\n**First 30 Days as a Stagehand Plan**\n\nWeek 1: Learn call etiquette, PPE, basic terms\n Week 2: Practice communication, case handling, cable awareness\n Week 3: Learn department names and basic gear\n Week 4: Ask leads for feedback and identify next skill path\n\n### **Key Outcome**\n\nLearners leave with an action plan, not just information.\n\n# **Suggested Final Course Structure**\n\nI would package it like this:\n\n| **Module** | **Title** | **Purpose** |\n| --- | --- | --- |\n| 1 | Welcome to the Live Event World | Understand the job and the industry |\n| 2 | Safety Mindset Before Skillset | OSHA-style awareness, rights, hazards |\n| 3 | PPE, Clothing, and What to Bring | Show up prepared |\n| 4 | Venue and Jobsite Awareness | Move safely through the worksite |\n| 5 | Load-In Fundamentals | Understand the beginning of the call |\n| 6 | Communication and Crew Etiquette | Learn how to be useful and professional |\n| 7 | Department Basics | Understand audio, lighting, video, staging, rigging awareness |\n| 8 | Tools, Gear, and Handling Basics | Recognize and handle common equipment |\n| 9 | Load-Out Fundamentals | Finish the call safely and effectively |\n| 10 | Getting Hired and Getting Called Back | Turn the training into real work |\n\n# **Course Promise**\n\nA strong course promise would be:\n\nBy the end of this course, learners will understand how live event crews work, how to prepare for a call, how to recognize common jobsite hazards, how load-ins and load-outs flow, how to communicate with leads, and how to show up as the kind of entry-level stagehand people want to call back.\n\nModule 1\n\nLesson 1\n\n## **Module 1, Lesson 1: What a Stagehand Actually Does**\n\n### **Lesson Purpose**\n\nThis lesson introduces new stagehands to the real purpose of the job. The goal is to help learners understand that stagehand work is not just physical labor. It is about supporting a larger production through safety, discipline, teamwork, and efficiency.\n\n### **Learning Objective**\n\nBy the end of this lesson, the learner should be able to explain what a stagehand does and why the role matters to the success of a live event.\n\n### **Main Teaching Points**\n\nA stagehand is one of the core support roles in live production. Stagehands help move, build, organize, set, strike, and reset the equipment that allows a concert, corporate event, theater production, festival, or live show to happen.\n\nThe job may include unloading trucks, pushing road cases, setting up staging, helping with cable runs, moving barricade, supporting lighting, audio, video, backline, or scenic departments, and cleaning or resetting work areas.\n\nBut the work is not just “moving boxes.”\n\nA good stagehand is paying attention, listening to direction, working safely, protecting the gear, respecting the crew structure, and helping the production move forward without creating problems.\n\nStagehands are the backbone of the production industry because almost every department depends on reliable hands to get gear from the truck to the stage and back again.\n\n### **Short Lesson Script**\n\nWhen people first hear the word “stagehand,” they often think the job is simple physical labor. They picture someone moving boxes, pushing cases, or carrying equipment.\n\nThat is part of the job, but it is not the whole job.\n\nA stagehand supports a larger production. That means your work affects the audio team, the lighting team, the video team, the rigging team, the stage manager, the touring crew, the venue, and the rest of the local crew.\n\nYour job is to help the production happen safely, efficiently, and professionally.\n\nAs a new stagehand, you may not know every piece of gear. You may not know every department. You may not know every term yet. That is okay.\n\nWhat matters most in the beginning is that you show up prepared, listen carefully, follow direction, ask questions when you are unsure, and work safely.\n\nThe best new stagehands are not the ones who try to prove they know everything. They are the ones who stay alert, stay useful, and understand that they are part of a much bigger team.\n\n### **Example Tasks a New Stagehand May Do**\n\n- Unload or reload trucks\n\n- Push road cases\n\n- Move barricade\n\n- Set chairs, risers, or deck pieces\n\n- Help keep pathways clear\n\n- Assist with cable runs under direction\n\n- Sort cases by department\n\n- Clean up tape, trash, and work areas\n\n- Help reset the venue after the event\n\n- Follow instructions from crew leads and department heads\n\n### **Key Concept**\n\nA stagehand’s value is not just strength. It is reliability, awareness, safety, and the ability to follow direction as part of a team.\n\n### **Reflection Question**\n\nBefore moving on, ask yourself:\n\nIf a lead asked me what a stagehand does, could I explain it without saying “we just move boxes”?\n\n### **Quick Knowledge Check**\n\n**Question:** What is the main role of an entry-level stagehand?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n To support the larger production by helping with safe, organized, and efficient labor under the direction of crew leads and department heads.\n\n### **Key Takeaway**\n\nA stagehand is the backbone of live production. The job requires physical work, but it also requires awareness, discipline, communication, and respect for the production process.\n\nLesson 2\n\n## **Module 1, Lesson 2: The Difference Between Load-In, Show Call, and Load-Out**\n\n### **Lesson Purpose**\n\nThis lesson helps new stagehands understand the three major types of event calls they may hear about in live production: **load-in, show call, and load-out**. The focus should stay beginner-friendly by making it clear that most new stagehands are more likely to start with **load-in and load-out**, not show call.\n\n### **Learning Objective**\n\nBy the end of this lesson, the learner should be able to explain the difference between load-in, show call, and load-out, and understand which parts of the process entry-level stagehands are most likely to support.\n\n## **Main Teaching Points**\n\n### **Load-In**\n\nLoad-in is the process of moving production equipment into a venue and building the show.\n\nThis can include unloading trucks, pushing road cases, setting up staging, moving barricade, helping departments get gear to the right place, running cable under direction, and preparing the space for the event.\n\nFor many new stagehands, **load-in is one of the first types of calls they will work**.\n\n### **Show Call**\n\nShow call refers to operational support during the actual event or performance.\n\nThis may include helping with scene changes, backstage movement, production support, resets, or other show-specific tasks. However, a brand-new stagehand may not be assigned to show call right away because show calls often require more trust, timing, experience, and knowledge of the production.\n\nFor this course, show call should be introduced as something to understand, but not the main focus.\n\n### **Load-Out**\n\nLoad-out is the process of striking, packing, and removing equipment after the event.\n\nLoad-out is usually faster and more physically intense than load-in. The crew is tired, the timeline is tight, and equipment needs to be packed correctly and moved back onto trucks. New stagehands are very likely to work load-outs, so safety, awareness, and following direction are especially important.\n\n## **Short Lesson Script**\n\nIn live production, not every call is the same. A stagehand may be called for load-in, load-out, or sometimes show call, depending on the event and their experience level.\n\n**Load-in** happens before the event. This is when the crew brings equipment into the venue and helps build the show. Trucks are unloaded, road cases are moved, staging may be built, cables may be run, and each department starts getting its gear into position.\n\n**Show call** happens during the actual performance or event. This type of call may involve backstage support, timing-specific tasks, or helping the production run while the audience is present. New stagehands should understand what show call means, but they should also know that beginners are not always placed there right away.\n\n**Load-out** happens after the event. This is when the crew takes everything down, packs equipment, clears the venue, and loads the trucks. Load-out can move quickly, but speed should never replace safety.\n\nFor a new stagehand, the most common starting point is usually load-in or load-out. These calls teach you how gear moves, how crews communicate, how departments work together, and how to be useful without getting in the way.\n\n## **Simple Comparison**\n\n| **Call Type** | **When It Happens** | **What It Means** | **Beginner Likelihood** |\n| --- | --- | --- | --- |\n| Load-In | Before the event | Gear comes in and the show gets built | Very likely |\n| Show Call | During the event | Crew supports the live performance | Less likely for brand-new stagehands |\n| Load-Out | After the event | Gear is struck, packed, and loaded out | Very likely |\n\n## **Example Tasks**\n\n### **Load-In Tasks**\n\n- Unload trucks\n\n- Push road cases\n\n- Sort gear by department\n\n- Move barricade or staging pieces\n\n- Help keep work areas clear\n\n- Follow directions from crew leads\n\n### **Show Call Tasks**\n\n- Stand by for assigned support\n\n- Assist with specific backstage changes\n\n- Help reset items during the event\n\n- Follow timing-sensitive instructions\n\n### **Load-Out Tasks**\n\n- Strike equipment\n\n- Pack road cases\n\n- Coil or move cable under direction\n\n- Push cases back to trucks\n\n- Clear trash, tape, and work areas\n\n- Help restore the venue\n\n## **Key Concept**\n\nLoad-in builds the show.\n Show call supports the show.\n Load-out takes the show apart and gets it back on the truck.\n\nFor beginners, the most important focus is learning how to work safely and effectively during **load-in and load-out**.\n\n## **Reflection Question**\n\nBefore moving on, ask yourself:\n\nIf someone told me I was booked for load-out, would I understand how that call is different from load-in?\n\n## **Quick Knowledge Check**\n\n**Question:** Which type of call is a new stagehand most likely to work first?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n A new stagehand is most likely to start with load-in or load-out because those calls often need general crew support, equipment movement, and basic labor under direction.\n\n## **Key Takeaway**\n\nUnderstanding the difference between load-in, show call, and load-out helps new stagehands know what kind of work to expect. Most beginners should focus first on being safe, useful, and reliable during load-in and load-out.\n\nLesson 3\n\n# **Lesson 3: Common Work Environments**\n\n## **Lesson Purpose**\n\nThis lesson introduces new stagehands to the different types of places they may work. The goal is to help learners understand that stagehand work changes depending on the venue, event type, crew size, schedule, and site conditions.\n\n## **Learning Objective**\n\nBy the end of this lesson, the learner should be able to identify common stagehand work environments and describe how each one affects the pace, expectations, and safety awareness required on the job.\n\n## **Main Teaching Points**\n\nStagehands do not work in only one kind of place. A call could be in a large arena, a small theater, a convention center, a hotel ballroom, a festival site, a stadium, or an outdoor temporary event space.\n\nThe basic expectations stay the same: show up prepared, work safely, follow direction, and support the production. But the environment changes how the work feels.\n\nSome environments are fast and loud. Some are quiet and precise. Some involve large crews and heavy equipment. Others require careful movement in tight backstage spaces. A good stagehand learns to adjust to the room.\n\n## **Short Lesson Script**\n\nStagehand work can happen in many different environments. Each one has its own pace, hazards, and expectations.\n\nArenas are large-scale, high-intensity environments. These calls often involve big trucks, large crews, heavy road cases, long pushes, forklifts, barricade, staging, audio, lighting, video, and rigging activity. The pace can be fast, and there may be a lot happening at once. In an arena, awareness is critical because equipment is constantly moving around you.\n\nTheaters are usually more precise. The spaces may be tighter, the backstage areas may be smaller, and the work may involve stage mechanics, curtains, fly systems, scenic pieces, props, or carefully timed changes. Theater work often requires patience, quiet communication, and attention to detail.\n\nIn theaters, beginners should never operate fly systems, stage machinery, lifts, automation, rigging lines, or rigging-related equipment unless they have been specifically trained and authorized.\n\nConvention centers can vary widely. One day may involve a trade show with booths, carpet, pipe and drape, signs, and freight. Another day may involve a large conference with staging, screens, audio, lighting, and breakout rooms. Convention work may involve long walks, repeated room setups, freight movement, room changes, and changing layouts.\n\nOutdoor shows add another layer of awareness. Weather, uneven ground, temporary structures, generators, fencing, tents, sun exposure, wind, dust, rain, and site logistics can all affect the work. Outdoor calls require extra attention to hydration, footwear, weather conditions, and safe movement around the site.\n\nIf weather conditions change, report concerns to a lead and follow site procedures. Do not attempt to secure tents, stages, electrical equipment, or temporary structures unless trained and assigned.\n\nAs a new stagehand, you do not need to know everything about every environment right away. What matters is learning how to observe the space, listen to your lead, identify hazards, and adjust your behavior to the jobsite you are in.\n\n## **Common Work Environments**\n\n| **Environment** | **What It’s Like** | **Beginner Focus** |\n| --- | --- | --- |\n| Arenas | Large, loud, fast-paced, heavy gear movement | Stay alert, watch for moving equipment, follow directions closely |\n| Theaters | More precise, often tighter backstage spaces | Move carefully, stay quiet when needed, respect stage systems |\n| Convention Centers | Wide variety of setups and layouts | Watch for freight movement, forklifts, pallet jacks, carts, room numbers, and traffic paths |\n| Outdoor Shows | Weather, terrain, temporary structures, site logistics | Hydrate, watch footing, prepare for changing conditions, report weather concerns |\n\n## **Example Beginner Tasks by Environment**\n\n## **Arena Tasks**\n\n- Push road cases\n\n- Move barricade when assigned\n\n- Help unload trucks under direction\n\n- Sort cases by department\n\n- Keep pathways clear\n\n- Stay aware of forklifts, carts, and moving equipment\n\n## **Theater Tasks**\n\n- Move scenic pieces carefully when assigned\n\n- Assist with soft goods or stage items under direction\n\n- Keep backstage areas organized\n\n- Follow quiet work expectations\n\n- Avoid touching stage machinery, fly systems, or rigging equipment unless trained and authorized\n\n## **Convention Center Tasks**\n\n- Set chairs or tables\n\n- Move pipe and drape under direction\n\n- Help with signage or room resets\n\n- Push freight or cases between rooms when assigned\n\n- Watch for forklifts, pallet jacks, carts, docks, and marked traffic paths\n\n## **Outdoor Show Tasks**\n\n- Move gear across uneven ground when assigned\n\n- Help with barricade, fencing, or staging support under direction\n\n- Watch for weather-related hazards\n\n- Keep water, shade, and footing in mind\n\n- Report unsafe weather, ground, or site conditions to a lead\n\n## **Important Safety Note**\n\nEach environment has different hazards. A beginner should not assume that a task is safe just because it looks simple. If you are not trained, assigned, or sure how to do something safely, stop and ask.\n\n## **Key Concept**\n\nThe job changes depending on the environment, but the professional expectations stay the same: be safe, listen, stay aware, and support the crew.\n\n## **Reflection Question**\n\nBefore moving on, ask yourself:\n\nHow would my behavior need to change between working in a loud arena and working backstage in a quiet theater?\n\n## **Quick Knowledge Check**\n\n**Question:** Why is it important for a stagehand to understand different work environments?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n Because each environment has different hazards, pace, crew expectations, and site conditions. A good stagehand adjusts to the space instead of treating every call the same.\n\n## **Key Takeaway**\n\nStagehands work in many different environments, including arenas, theaters, convention centers, and outdoor sites. Each environment has unique challenges, but every call requires safety, awareness, communication, and the ability to follow direction.\n\nLesson 4\n\n## **Module 1, Lesson 4: Who Is on the Crew**\n\n### **Lesson Purpose**\n\nThis lesson introduces new stagehands to the different people they may work around on a live event crew. The goal is to help learners understand who gives direction, who performs specialized work, and why entry-level stagehands should respect the crew structure instead of guessing or freelancing.\n\n### **Learning Objective**\n\nBy the end of this lesson, the learner should be able to identify common crew roles and understand who to listen to when receiving work direction.\n\n## **Main Teaching Points**\n\nA live event crew is made up of different roles and departments. Each group has a specific job, and those jobs have to work together for the production to happen safely and efficiently.\n\nAs a new stagehand, your job is not to know everything about every department. Your job is to understand the basic crew structure, know who your point of contact is, and follow direction from the correct people.\n\n## **Short Lesson Script**\n\nWhen you arrive at a call, you are stepping into a team environment. There may be dozens or even hundreds of people working around you, depending on the size of the event.\n\nThe most important people for a new stagehand to identify are the **leads and department heads**. These are the people who give direction, assign tasks, answer questions, and keep the work organized. If you are unsure what to do, you should look for your lead instead of guessing.\n\n**Riggers** are specialists who work with overhead equipment. They may be involved with motors, truss, points, steel, and anything that gets flown above the stage or audience area. As a beginner, you should never interfere with rigging work or walk into rigging areas without direction. Overhead work requires serious awareness and specialized training.\n\nYou will also see department-specific specialists, such as **audio, lighting, video, and carpentry crews**. These teams are responsible for their own equipment and systems. Audio may handle speakers, consoles, microphones, and cable. Lighting may handle fixtures, truss, dimmers, and control. Video may handle screens, cameras, projectors, and LED walls. Carpenters may handle staging, scenic pieces, decks, platforms, and set elements.\n\nAnother important group is the **truck loaders**. Truck loaders focus on the logistics of getting gear in and out of trailers safely and efficiently. They may direct how cases are stacked, what order gear moves, and where equipment needs to go. Truck loading is not random. Gear usually has a specific order and placement.\n\nAs a new stagehand, remember this: do not assume that every person on-site can give you work direction. Know who your lead is, follow the chain of command, and ask questions when you are unsure.\n\n## **Common Crew Roles**\n\n| **Crew Role** | **What They Do** | **What Beginners Should Know** |\n| --- | --- | --- |\n| Leads / Department Heads | Give direction and organize the work | They are your main point of contact |\n| Riggers | Handle overhead systems and flown equipment | Stay clear unless directed |\n| Audio Crew | Handle sound equipment and audio systems | Do not touch audio gear unless asked |\n| Lighting Crew | Handle lighting fixtures, truss, cable, and control | Follow direction carefully around lighting gear |\n| Video Crew | Handle screens, cameras, projectors, and LED systems | Video equipment can be delicate and expensive |\n| Carpenters / Staging Crew | Handle decks, scenic pieces, platforms, and stage structure | Pay attention to safe lifting and placement |\n| Truck Loaders | Manage gear movement in and out of trailers | Follow truck direction and do not stack gear randomly |\n\n## **Example Beginner Situations**\n\n### **Situation 1**\n\nYou are standing near a stack of road cases and are not sure where they go.\n\n**Best action:** Ask your lead where the cases should be moved.\n\n### **Situation 2**\n\nYou see lighting crew working on truss nearby.\n\n**Best action:** Stay clear and wait for direction. Do not walk into their work area or touch gear.\n\n### **Situation 3**\n\nSomeone near the truck tells you to push a case into the trailer.\n\n**Best action:** Make sure they are part of the truck loading team or confirm with your lead before moving gear into the truck.\n\n### **Situation 4**\n\nYou see a cable on the ground and want to help clean it up.\n\n**Best action:** Ask before coiling or moving it. Some cables may still be in use or need to be handled a specific way.\n\n## **Key Concept**\n\nA production crew works through structure and communication. New stagehands should know who their lead is, follow direction, and avoid touching specialized gear without permission.\n\n## **Reflection Question**\n\nBefore moving on, ask yourself:\n\nIf I am unsure what to do on a call, who should I ask first?\n\n## **Quick Knowledge Check**\n\n**Question:** Why should a new stagehand avoid taking direction from random people on-site?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n Because live event crews have a chain of command. Taking direction from the wrong person can create confusion, move gear to the wrong place, slow down the crew, or create a safety issue.\n\n## **Key Takeaway**\n\nEvery person on the crew has a role. Leads and department heads give direction, riggers handle overhead work, departments manage their own equipment, and truck loaders control trailer logistics. As a new stagehand, your job is to listen, follow the correct chain of command, and support the crew safely.\n\nLesson 5\n\n# **Lesson 5: What Makes a New Stagehand Valuable**\n\n## **Lesson Purpose**\n\nThis lesson teaches new stagehands what crews actually look for in beginners. The goal is to help learners understand that they do not need to know everything on day one. What matters most is being reliable, safe, coachable, and easy to work with.\n\n## **Learning Objective**\n\nBy the end of this lesson, the learner should be able to identify the habits and behaviors that make an entry-level stagehand valuable on a live event crew.\n\n## **Main Teaching Points**\n\nA new stagehand is not expected to know every department, every piece of gear, or every production term right away.\n\nWhat makes a new stagehand valuable is not advanced technical knowledge. It is the ability to show up on time, listen carefully, follow instructions, communicate clearly, and work safely.\n\nA valuable stagehand follows instructions precisely, while also stopping to ask questions when something is unclear, unsafe, or outside their training.\n\nCrew leads want beginners who can be trusted with basic tasks and who do not create extra problems for the team.\n\n## **Short Lesson Script**\n\nWhen you are new to stagehand work, it is easy to think you need to know everything right away. You might feel pressure to prove yourself, move fast, or act like you understand more than you do.\n\nThat is not what makes a new stagehand valuable.\n\nA valuable new stagehand is reliable. That means you show up on time, ready to work, dressed correctly, and prepared for the call. If the call time is 8:00, that does not mean walking in at 8:00 looking for parking or trying to find the entrance. It means being checked in, ready, and available for direction.\n\nA valuable new stagehand listens actively. When a lead gives instructions, you pay attention, make eye contact when appropriate, and confirm what you heard. If you do not understand something, you ask before acting.\n\nA valuable new stagehand communicates clearly. You do not disappear, guess, or pretend. You let your lead know when a task is done, when you are unsure, or when you see something that may be unsafe.\n\nMost importantly, a valuable new stagehand is committed to safety. Moving fast is never more important than protecting people, gear, and the venue. A good beginner understands that safety is part of the job, not something separate from the job.\n\nBeing valuable does not mean saying yes to every task. If you are not trained, authorized, or sure how to do something safely, stop and ask.\n\nThe best new stagehands are not the loudest or the strongest. They are the ones who can be trusted to listen, work carefully, follow direction, communicate clearly, and improve throughout the call.\n\n## **What Makes a New Stagehand Valuable**\n\n| **Valuable Trait** | **What It Looks Like on the Job** |\n| --- | --- |\n| Reliability | Showing up prepared and ready to work |\n| Punctuality | Being early enough to check in, find your lead, and receive direction |\n| Active Listening | Paying attention, confirming instructions, and not interrupting |\n| Clear Communication | Asking questions, reporting problems, and updating your lead |\n| Safety Commitment | Watching for hazards and not rushing into unsafe actions |\n| Following Instructions | Doing the task exactly as directed before trying to improvise |\n| Good Judgment | Stopping to ask when something is unclear, unsafe, or outside your training |\n\n## **Examples of Valuable Beginner Behavior**\n\n## **Good Example 1**\n\nA lead says, “Take these four cases to stage left and leave them closed.”\n\nA valuable new stagehand moves the correct cases to stage left, leaves them closed, and checks back in when finished.\n\n## **Good Example 2**\n\nA new stagehand does not know whether a cable is safe to move.\n\nA valuable new stagehand asks, “Do you want this cable moved, or should I leave it until the department clears it?”\n\n## **Good Example 3**\n\nA crew member finishes a task and is not sure what to do next.\n\nA valuable new stagehand checks in with the lead instead of wandering, standing in the way, or starting random tasks.\n\n## **Good Example 4**\n\nA new stagehand is asked to help with something they have not been trained to do.\n\nA valuable new stagehand says, “I have not been trained on that yet. Can someone show me the safe way, or would you like me to help with something else?”\n\n## **Bad Example**\n\nA new stagehand sees a road case and decides to move it without asking.\n\nThat may seem helpful, but it can cause problems if the case was already staged correctly, belongs to another department, or is needed in that exact spot.\n\n## **Important Safety Note**\n\nFollowing instructions is important, but safety still matters. If an instruction is unclear, seems unsafe, or involves work you have not been trained or authorized to do, stop and ask for clarification.\n\n## **Key Concept**\n\nA new stagehand becomes valuable by being dependable, safe, attentive, honest, and coachable. You do not need to know everything. You need to listen, communicate, follow direction, and ask questions when needed.\n\n## **Reflection Question**\n\nBefore moving on, ask yourself:\n\nIf I were a crew lead, would I rather work with someone who knows a little but listens well, or someone who acts confident but does not follow direction?\n\n## **Quick Knowledge Check**\n\n**Question:** What is one of the most valuable traits for a new stagehand?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n Reliability. A new stagehand who shows up on time, listens carefully, works safely, communicates clearly, and follows instructions is more useful than someone who tries to act experienced but creates confusion.\n\n## **Key Takeaway**\n\nA valuable new stagehand is reliable, punctual, safe, honest, and easy to direct. Beginners are not expected to know everything, but they are expected to listen, communicate clearly, follow instructions precisely, and ask questions when something is unclear, unsafe, or outside their training.\n\nEnd of Module 1 Quiz\n\n# **Module 1 Quiz: Welcome to Stagehand Work**\n\n## **Quiz Instructions**\n\nChoose the best answer for each question. This quiz checks your understanding of what a stagehand does, common types of calls, work environments, crew structure, and the habits that make a new stagehand valuable.\n\n**Recommended passing score:** 80%\n **Total questions:** 20\n\n## **Multiple Choice**\n\n### **1. What is the main role of an entry-level stagehand?**\n\nA. To run the entire production\n B. To support the larger production through safe, organized, and efficient labor\n C. To operate all technical equipment\n D. To make independent decisions without asking leads\n\n**Correct Answer:** B\n\n### **2. Why is stagehand work more than “moving boxes”?**\n\nA. Because stagehands are responsible for selling tickets\n B. Because stagehands support the larger production through safety, teamwork, communication, and discipline\n C. Because stagehands only work during the show\n D. Because stagehands only work in theaters\n\n**Correct Answer:** B\n\n### **3. Which of the following is a realistic beginner stagehand task?**\n\nA. Operating rigging motors without supervision\n B. Rewiring electrical systems\n C. Pushing road cases to assigned locations\n D. Calling cues for the show\n\n**Correct Answer:** C\n\n### **4. What should a new stagehand do if they are unsure whether something is safe to move?**\n\nA. Move it quickly before anyone notices\n B. Ask a lead or appropriate department before acting\n C. Ignore it and leave the area\n D. Ask another new stagehand\n\n**Correct Answer:** B\n\n### **5. What is load-in?**\n\nA. The process of removing gear after the event\n B. The process of moving gear into a venue and building the show\n C. The live performance itself\n D. The break between shifts\n\n**Correct Answer:** B\n\n### **6. What is load-out?**\n\nA. The process of striking, packing, organizing, and removing equipment after the event\n B. The process of selling merchandise\n C. The first meeting before the crew starts work\n D. The process of rehearsing the show\n\n**Correct Answer:** A\n\n### **7. Why are brand-new stagehands less likely to be assigned to show call right away?**\n\nA. Show calls often require more trust, timing, experience, and production knowledge\n B. Show calls do not need stagehands\n C. Show calls only happen outdoors\n D. Show calls are always unpaid\n\n**Correct Answer:** A\n\n### **8. During load-out, what should never replace safety?**\n\nA. Communication\n B. Teamwork\n C. Speed\n D. Direction from a lead\n\n**Correct Answer:** C\n\n### **9. Which work environment is usually large-scale, loud, fast-paced, and often involves heavy gear movement?**\n\nA. Theater\n B. Arena\n C. Classroom\n D. Office\n\n**Correct Answer:** B\n\n### **10. Which environment may involve tighter backstage spaces, stage mechanics, scenic pieces, and quiet communication?**\n\nA. Theater\n B. Outdoor festival site\n C. Warehouse\n D. Parking lot\n\n**Correct Answer:** A\n\n### **11. What is especially important at outdoor shows?**\n\nA. Ignoring the weather\n B. Weather awareness, hydration, footing, and site logistics\n C. Wearing white formal clothing\n D. Working without checking in\n\n**Correct Answer:** B\n\n### **12. What should a beginner do if weather conditions change at an outdoor show?**\n\nA. Try to secure tents or structures alone\n B. Report concerns to a lead and follow site procedures\n C. Leave the site immediately without telling anyone\n D. Move electrical equipment on their own\n\n**Correct Answer:** B\n\n### **13. Who is usually the new stagehand’s primary point of contact for direction?**\n\nA. Audience members\n B. Leads and department heads\n C. Vendors\n D. Anyone standing nearby\n\n**Correct Answer:** B\n\n### **14. What do riggers specialize in?**\n\nA. Ticketing and guest services\n B. Overhead equipment, flown systems, motors, truss, and rigging-related work\n C. Catering\n D. Marketing\n\n**Correct Answer:** B\n\n### **15. What should a new stagehand do around rigging zones?**\n\nA. Stand under suspended loads to watch\n B. Touch motors to help speed things up\n C. Stay clear, never stand under suspended loads, and wait for direction\n D. Move rigging hardware without asking\n\n**Correct Answer:** C\n\n### **16. Why should a new stagehand avoid taking direction from random people on-site?**\n\nA. Because live event crews use a chain of command\n B. Because no one is allowed to talk on-site\n C. Because all instructions are optional\n D. Because beginners should work independently\n\n**Correct Answer:** A\n\n### **17. Which trait makes a new stagehand valuable?**\n\nA. Acting like they already know everything\n B. Guessing when instructions are unclear\n C. Reliability and punctuality\n D. Ignoring safety to move faster\n\n**Correct Answer:** C\n\n### **18. What does active listening look like on a call?**\n\nA. Interrupting the lead\n B. Paying attention, confirming instructions, and asking questions when needed\n C. Walking away during instructions\n D. Pretending to understand\n\n**Correct Answer:** B\n\n### **19. What should a stagehand do if they are asked to perform a task they have not been trained or authorized to do?**\n\nA. Try it anyway\n B. Let another beginner show them\n C. Stop and ask for clarification or proper direction\n D. Walk away without saying anything\n\n**Correct Answer:** C\n\n### **20. Which statement best describes a valuable new stagehand?**\n\nA. Someone who works fast, even if they are unsafe\n B. Someone who follows direction, communicates clearly, works safely, and asks questions when needed\n C. Someone who moves gear without asking\n D. Someone who avoids checking in with their lead\n\n**Correct Answer:** B\n\n# **Scenario Questions**\n\nThese can be used as written-response questions or discussion prompts.\n\n## **Scenario 1**\n\nYou see a road case sitting near the stage. No one has told you to move it, but it looks like it might be in the way.\n\n**Question:** What should you do?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n Do not move it without direction. Check with your lead or the appropriate department to confirm whether it should stay there or be moved.\n\n## **Scenario 2**\n\nYou are working load-out. The crew is moving quickly, and someone tells you to grab a cable and pull it across the stage. You are not sure what it is connected to.\n\n**Question:** What should you do?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n Stop and ask before pulling it. Cable should only be moved or coiled under direction from the appropriate department, especially if you are unsure whether it is still connected or in use.\n\n## **Scenario 3**\n\nYou arrive at a venue and do not know who your lead is.\n\n**Question:** What should you do?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n Check in and ask who you should report to. A new stagehand should identify their lead or department contact before starting work.\n\n## **Scenario 4**\n\nYou see riggers working overhead and a suspended load above the work area.\n\n**Question:** What should you do?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n Stay clear of the rigging zone, never stand under suspended loads, and wait for direction from the appropriate lead.\n\n## **Scenario 5**\n\nA lead gives you instructions, but you only understood part of what they said.\n\n**Question:** What should you do?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n Ask for clarification before starting the task. It is better to ask a question than to guess and create confusion or a safety issue.\n\n# **Answer Key**\n\n- B\n\n- B\n\n- C\n\n- B\n\n- B\n\n- A\n\n- A\n\n- C\n\n- B\n\n- A\n\n- B\n\n- B\n\n- B\n\n- B\n\n- C\n\n- A\n\n- C\n\n- B\n\n- C\n\n- B\n\nModule 2\n\nLesson 2.1\n\n# **Module 2, Lesson 1: Why Safety Comes Before Speed**\n\n## **Lesson Purpose**\n\nThis lesson teaches new stagehands that safety is the foundation of professional stagehand work.\n\nThe goal is to help learners understand that moving fast is not valuable if it creates injuries, damages gear, blocks the workflow, or puts other crew members at risk.\n\nA good stagehand is not just fast. A good stagehand is safe, aware, reliable, and controlled.\n\n## **Learning Objective**\n\nBy the end of this lesson, the learner should be able to explain why safety must come before speed on a live event jobsite and identify common situations where slowing down, asking questions, or waiting for direction is the professional choice.\n\n## **Main Teaching Points**\n\nLive event work often moves quickly. Load-ins, show calls, and load-outs can involve tight schedules, heavy equipment, moving vehicles, crowded work areas, low light, fatigue, noise, and pressure from the clock.\n\nBecause of that pressure, new stagehands may think they need to move as fast as possible to prove themselves.\n\nThat mindset can be dangerous.\n\nSpeed without awareness can cause injuries, damaged gear, blocked pathways, dropped equipment, miscommunication, and delays. A rushed worker can create more problems than they solve.\n\nSafety comes before speed because people matter more than the schedule. Gear can be replaced. A call can be adjusted. But injuries can change someone’s life.\n\nEmployers are responsible for providing a workplace free from serious recognized hazards and for giving safety training in language and vocabulary workers can understand. Workers also have a responsibility to follow safety rules, use required PPE, ask questions, and report unsafe conditions.\n\nFor a new stagehand, safety does not mean being slow, lazy, or afraid to work. It means working with control.\n\nMove efficiently, not recklessly.\n\nThat means looking before moving, listening before acting, and asking before guessing.\n\n## **Short Lesson Script**\n\nIn live production, everything can feel urgent.\n\nThe truck needs to be unloaded. The stage needs to be built. The barricade needs to move. The cable needs to get across the room.
