The show clock is always running.\n\nBecause of that, new stagehands sometimes think the best way to stand out is to move as fast as possible.\n\nBut speed is not the first priority.\n\nSafety is.\n\nA fast stagehand who ignores hazards can hurt someone, damage expensive equipment, block the crew, or slow the entire production down. A stagehand who rushes without thinking might push a case into someone, trip over cable, step into a forklift path, grab gear that is not ready to move, or walk under overhead work.\n\nThat is not being helpful.\n\nA professional stagehand moves with purpose, not panic.\n\nThere is a difference between working efficiently and rushing. Working efficiently means you listen, understand the task, move with control, stay aware of your surroundings, and check back in when the task is done.\n\nRushing means you move before you understand, ignore what is around you, and hope nothing goes wrong.\n\nOn a live event jobsite, hope is not a safety plan.\n\nSafety comes before speed because every person on the crew is depending on the people around them to make smart choices. Audio, lighting, video, rigging, staging, truck loaders, local crew, touring crew, venue staff, and security all share the same work environment.\n\nYour actions affect more than just you.\n\nIf you are pushing a road case, you need to know where you are going, who is around you, whether the path is clear, and whether the case is safe to move.\n\nIf you see a cable, you should not pull it unless you have been told to. It may still be connected, in use, or part of another department’s system.\n\nIf a forklift is moving nearby, you should stay clear and follow site procedures before crossing a path.\n\nIf people are working overhead, you should never assume the area underneath is safe. Stay clear unless a lead directs you otherwise.\n\nIf you are tired during load-out, you need to be even more aware, not less. Fatigue and speed are a dangerous combination.\n\nBeing safe does not mean you are weak. It does not mean you are slow. It means you are professional.\n\nThe stagehands who get asked back are not the ones who run around trying to look busy. They are the ones who can be trusted to work hard without creating problems.\n\nWhen you are new, your best move is simple:\n\nListen first.\n Look around.\n Move with control.\n Ask when unsure.\n Never let speed replace safety.\n\n## **Speed vs. Safety Comparison**\n\n| **Situation** | **Unsafe “Fast” Choice** | **Better Professional Choice** |\n| --- | --- | --- |\n| A road case needs to move | Push it quickly without checking the path | Check the path, communicate with nearby crew, and move with control |\n| Cable is lying across the floor | Grab it and pull it out of the way | Ask the lead or department before moving it |\n| Forklift is nearby | Walk behind it to save time | Stay clear and wait until the travel path is safe |\n| Load-out is moving fast | Run with gear or rush down ramps | Keep control, use proper help, and follow direction |\n| You do not understand the task | Pretend you understand and start anyway | Ask for clarification before acting |\n| A lead says “hurry up” | Panic and stop watching your surroundings | Move efficiently while still staying aware and safe |\n\n## **Beginner Safety Mindset**\n\nA new stagehand should think:\n\n“I am here to help the crew, not create extra problems.”\n\nThat means:\n\n● I will not move gear unless I understand where it is going.\n ● I will not touch department equipment unless directed.\n ● I will not run with cases, tools, cable, or gear.\n ● I will watch for forklifts, carts, pallet jacks, pushers, and moving cases.\n ● I will stay clear of overhead work and suspended loads.\n ● I will keep exits, fire lanes, ramps, docks, and pathways clear.\n ● I will ask questions when something is unclear or unsafe.\n ● I will report hazards instead of ignoring them.\n ● I will remember that being safe is part of being useful.\n\n## **Common “Speed Traps” for New Stagehands**\n\n### **Trying to Prove Yourself**\n\nNew workers often want to show they are strong, fast, and ready. That is understandable, but trying too hard can lead to mistakes.\n\nA better goal is to be dependable and coachable.\n\n### **Copying Unsafe Behavior**\n\nYou may see someone else rushing, climbing, lifting poorly, skipping PPE, or moving through a restricted area. Do not assume it is okay just because someone else does it.\n\nFollow your lead, your employer’s rules, site procedures, and your training.\n\n### **Acting Before Understanding**\n\nIf you only understood half of the instruction, pause and ask. A short question is better than moving the wrong case, damaging gear, or putting someone in danger.\n\n### **Load-Out Pressure**\n\nLoad-out often feels faster than load-in. Everyone is tired, the crowd may have just left, trucks are waiting, and the crew wants to finish.\n\nThat is exactly when safety matters most.\n\n### **Poor Awareness**\n\nMany beginner mistakes happen because someone is focused only on the thing in front of them. Stagehands need wide awareness. That means paying attention to the floor, overhead work, moving equipment, nearby crew, cables, ramps, dock areas, and changing conditions.\n\n## **Example Beginner Situations**\n\n### **Situation 1**\n\nYou are told to move a road case across the arena floor. The shortest path crosses in front of a forklift.\n\nBest action: Do not cut across the forklift path. Wait, make sure the path is clear, and move the case only when it is safe.\n\n### **Situation 2**\n\nDuring load-out, someone says, “Grab that cable and pull it back.”\n\nYou are not sure what the cable is connected to.\n\nBest action: Pause and ask before pulling. Cable should only be moved under proper direction, especially if you do not know whether it is still connected or in use.\n\n### **Situation 3**\n\nYou are pushing a case and another crew member walks into your path.\n\nBest action: Slow down, communicate clearly, and keep control of the case. Do not assume they see you.\n\n### **Situation 4**\n\nA lead gives instructions quickly, and you are not sure where the gear should go.\n\nBest action: Ask for clarification before moving the gear. Guessing can waste time or create a hazard.\n\n### **Situation 5**\n\nThe crew is moving quickly during load-out, and you feel pressure to run with a small piece of gear.\n\nBest action: Do not run. Move with purpose and control. Running on a jobsite increases the chance of slips, trips, collisions, and dropped equipment.\n\n## **Important Safety Note**\n\nThis lesson is safety-awareness training for entry-level stagehands. It does not replace employer-provided safety training, site-specific instruction, union training, equipment certification, or OSHA 10/30 training.\n\nAlways follow your employer’s rules, venue procedures, lead instructions, posted warnings, and required PPE policies. If something seems unsafe, unclear, or outside your training, pause your own work, move to a safe position if needed, and ask the correct lead.\n\n## **Key Concept**\n\nSpeed is only valuable when it is controlled. A stagehand who moves quickly but creates hazards is not helping. A stagehand who works safely, communicates clearly, and follows direction is much more valuable to the crew.\n\nMove efficiently, not recklessly.\n\n## **Reflection Question**\n\nBefore moving on, ask yourself:\n\nHave I ever tried to move faster than I could safely control? What could happen if I did that on a live event jobsite?\n\n## **Quick Knowledge Check**\n\n**Question:** Why should safety come before speed during stagehand work?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n Because rushing can cause injuries, damage equipment, create confusion, and slow down the production. A professional stagehand works efficiently, but never lets speed replace awareness, communication, control, or safety.\n\n## **Key Takeaway**\n\nLive event work can move fast, but safety must always come first. New stagehands should focus on controlled movement, awareness, clear communication, and asking questions when unsure. The goal is not to look busy. The goal is to help the crew safely and professionally.\n\nLesson 2.2\n\n# **Module 2: Safety Mindset Before Skillset**\n\n## **Module Safety Disclaimer**\n\nThis module provides entry-level safety awareness for live event stagehand work. It is not OSHA 10/30 training, legal advice, employer-specific training, union training, site-specific orientation, equipment certification, rigging training, forklift training, lift training, fall-protection certification, electrical training, or medical guidance.\n\nThis module discusses general U.S. OSHA worker-safety concepts. Exact responsibilities may vary depending on employer, jurisdiction, work arrangement, venue policy, contract requirements, and site-specific procedures.\n\nAlways follow your employer’s safety program, venue rules, site-specific procedures, posted warnings, required PPE rules, and instructions from qualified leads or supervisors. OSHA states that employers are responsible for providing a workplace free from serious recognized hazards and providing safety training in language and vocabulary workers can understand.\n\nIf something is unclear, unsafe, or outside your training, pause your own work, move to a safe position if needed, and ask the correct lead before continuing.\n\n## **Standard Safety Phrase for Module 2**\n\nIf you are not trained, authorized, assigned, and sure how to do the task safely, pause before acting and ask the correct lead.\n\n# **Module 2, Lesson 2: Worker Rights and Employer Responsibilities**\n\n## **Lesson Purpose**\n\nThis lesson introduces new stagehands to the basic idea that safety is a shared responsibility.\n\nEmployers have responsibilities to provide a safe workplace, proper training, required safety equipment, and clear procedures.\n\nWorkers also have responsibilities to follow safety rules, use required PPE, report hazards, and ask questions when something is unclear or unsafe.\n\nThe goal is not to turn learners into legal experts. The goal is to help them understand that safety is not optional, and that speaking up about a real hazard is part of being professional.\n\n## **Learning Objective**\n\nBy the end of this lesson, the learner should be able to identify basic worker rights, recognize general employer safety responsibilities, and explain how a new stagehand should respond when they see an unsafe condition on a live event jobsite.\n\n## **Main Teaching Points**\n\nA live event jobsite can include moving trucks, forklifts, road cases, cables, temporary structures, overhead rigging, loud environments, poor lighting, tight schedules, and tired crews.\n\nBecause of these risks, workers need to understand both their rights and their responsibilities.\n\nWorkers have the right to a workplace that is free from serious recognized hazards. They also have the right to receive safety and health training in language they understand, receive required safety equipment, report injuries or illnesses, and raise safety concerns without retaliation. OSHA’s worker-safety materials support these general rights and responsibilities.\n\nEmployers are responsible for providing a safe workplace, making sure tools and equipment are safe and properly maintained, using signs or labels to warn about hazards, communicating safety procedures, and providing safety training in language and vocabulary workers can understand.\n\nRequired PPE rules can depend on the job, employer, venue, and applicable standard. In many OSHA-covered situations, employers are responsible for required PPE, though OSHA allows exceptions for some items such as ordinary clothing and certain non-specialty safety-toe footwear. For this course, learners should follow their employer’s PPE policy and ask before entering any area where PPE may be required. OSHA’s PPE standards also require employers to train employees who must use PPE on when PPE is necessary, what PPE is necessary, how to wear it, its limitations, and its care.\n\nWorkers also have responsibilities. A stagehand should follow jobsite rules, use required PPE, stay out of restricted areas, report hazards, follow direction from leads, and avoid doing tasks they have not been trained or authorized to do.\n\nFor new stagehands, this means one thing clearly:\n\nYou are allowed to ask questions when something seems unsafe.\n\n## **Short Lesson Script**\n\nWhen you walk onto a live event jobsite, safety is not just one person’s job.\n\nIt is not only the safety manager’s job.\n It is not only the crew lead’s job.\n It is not only the employer’s job.\n And it is not only your job.\n\nSafety works best when everyone understands their role.\n\nEmployers have responsibilities. They are responsible for providing a workplace that is as safe as possible, giving workers information about hazards, providing required safety training, and making sure workers know what equipment they need for the hazards of the job.\n\nThat matters in live events because the work can change from site to site. One day you may be in an arena pushing road cases across a concrete floor. Another day you may be outside dealing with weather, uneven ground, temporary power, and heavy equipment. Another day you may be in a theater with tight backstage spaces and overhead systems.\n\nDifferent jobsites have different hazards, so site-specific training and instructions matter.\n\nAs a worker, you also have responsibilities. You need to listen during safety briefings, follow instructions, wear required PPE, use equipment only as directed, and speak up when something seems unsafe.\n\nThis does not mean you should argue with every instruction or refuse tasks just because you are nervous. It means you should pay attention and be honest when something is unclear, unsafe, or outside your training.\n\nFor example, if someone tells you to move a road case, but you do not know where it goes, ask.\n\nIf someone tells you to pull a cable, but you do not know whether it is still connected, ask.\n\nIf you are told to enter an area where riggers are working overhead, pause and check with your lead.\n\nIf you are asked to operate equipment you have not been trained or authorized to use, say so.\n\nA professional stagehand does not pretend. A professional stagehand communicates.\n\nSpeaking up does not have to be dramatic. You can say:\n\n“I am not sure that path is clear.”\n “I have not been trained on that.”\n “Can you show me the safe way to do it?”\n “Should we check with the lead before moving this?”\n “There is a wet spot near the cable path.”\n “There is a case blocking the exit path.”\n\nThese are not complaints. These are safety communications.\n\nThe best crews want workers who notice problems before someone gets hurt.\n\nAs a new stagehand, you may feel pressure to stay quiet because you do not want to look inexperienced. But asking a safety question is not weakness. It shows awareness.\n\nYou do not need to know everything on day one. You do need to know that safety concerns should be reported, unclear instructions should be clarified, and unsafe shortcuts should not be ignored.\n\n## **Worker Rights: Beginner-Friendly Version**\n\n| **Worker Right** | **What It Means for a Stagehand** |\n| --- | --- |\n| Right to a safe workplace | The jobsite should be managed to reduce serious hazards |\n| Right to safety training | You should receive safety information you can understand |\n| Right to required safety equipment | PPE requirements should be clearly explained and handled according to employer policy and applicable standards |\n| Right to report hazards | You can speak up when something looks unsafe |\n| Right to report injuries | Injuries and illnesses should be reported through the proper process |\n| Right to ask questions | You can ask for clarification when instructions are unclear or unsafe |\n| Right to be free from retaliation | Workers should not be punished for raising legitimate safety concerns |\n\n## **Employer Responsibilities: Beginner-Friendly Version**\n\n| **Employer Responsibility** | **What It May Look Like on a Live Event Jobsite** |\n| --- | --- |\n| Provide a safe workplace | Managing known hazards and keeping work areas reasonably safe |\n| Provide safety training | Explaining site hazards, emergency procedures, PPE, and work expectations |\n| Communicate procedures | Giving safety briefings, call instructions, restricted area rules, and emergency plans |\n| Provide or require PPE according to applicable rules | Making sure workers know what PPE is required and how to use it properly |\n| Maintain safe tools and equipment | Keeping work equipment in safe operating condition |\n| Warn workers about hazards | Using signs, barricades, cones, verbal warnings, briefings, or lead instructions |\n| Respond to reported hazards | Taking concerns seriously and correcting or controlling hazards when possible |\n\n## **Worker Responsibilities: Beginner-Friendly Version**\n\nA new stagehand should:\n\n● Listen during safety briefings\n ● Follow instructions from the correct lead or department head\n ● Wear required PPE correctly\n ● Stay out of restricted areas unless assigned\n ● Avoid touching gear without direction\n ● Report hazards, injuries, and near misses\n ● Ask questions when unsure\n ● Use tools and equipment only as trained and authorized\n ● Keep pathways, exits, docks, ramps, and work areas clear\n ● Avoid horseplay, rushing, freelancing, or unsafe shortcuts\n\n## **What This Means on a Stagehand Call**\n\n### **You Have the Right to Ask**\n\nIf you do not understand an instruction, ask for clarification.\n\nExample:\n “Can you tell me where this case should land?”\n\n### **You Have the Responsibility to Listen**\n\nOnce the lead gives you the instruction, follow it carefully.\n\nExample:\n If the lead says, “Take this to stage right and leave it closed,” do not open it, move it somewhere else, or hand it off without checking.\n\n### **You Have the Right to Report Hazards**\n\nIf you see something unsafe, report it to your lead or the proper person.\n\nExample:\n “There is a wet spot by the cable path near stage left.”\n\n### **You Have the Responsibility Not to Create Hazards**\n\nDo not leave cases in walkways, block exits, drop trash, run with gear, or move cables without direction.\n\n### **You Have the Right to Required Safety Information**\n\nIf the job involves specific hazards, workers should be told how to recognize and avoid them. OSHA emphasizes training in language and vocabulary workers can understand.\n\n### **You Have the Responsibility to Be Honest About Your Training**\n\nIf you have not been trained on a task, say so.\n\nExample:\n “I have not been trained on that yet. Can someone show me the safe way, or would you like me to help with something else?”\n\n## **Common Beginner Mistakes**\n\n### **Mistake 1: Staying Silent Because You Are New**\n\nNew stagehands sometimes see a hazard but stay quiet because they do not want to look inexperienced.\n\nBetter choice: Report the concern calmly to your lead.\n\n### **Mistake 2: Thinking Safety Is Someone Else’s Job**\n\nSafety managers, leads, and employers have major responsibilities, but every worker affects the jobsite.\n\nBetter choice: Stay aware of your own actions and your surroundings.\n\n### **Mistake 3: Confusing Confidence With Authorization**\n\nJust because you feel capable does not mean you are trained or authorized.\n\nBetter choice: Do not operate lifts, forklifts, rigging equipment, power systems, stage machinery, or specialized tools unless properly trained and assigned.\n\n### **Mistake 4: Ignoring Required PPE**\n\nPPE only helps when it is used correctly.\n\nBetter choice: Wear required PPE according to jobsite rules and ask if you are unsure what is required.\n\n### **Mistake 5: Reporting Too Late**\n\nA hazard reported after someone gets hurt is no longer prevention.\n\nBetter choice: Speak up early when you notice a serious hazard.\n\n## **Example Beginner Situations**\n\n### **Situation 1**\n\nYou arrive at a venue and no one has explained where emergency exits, restricted areas, or check-in locations are.\n\nBest action: Ask your lead where you should report, what areas are restricted, and whether there is a safety briefing.\n\n### **Situation 2**\n\nYou are told to move cases through a path where a forklift is actively working.\n\nBest action: Pause and wait for clear direction. Do not walk or push cases through active equipment paths unless it is safe and directed.\n\n### **Situation 3**\n\nYou are asked to help with a task involving equipment you have never used before.\n\nBest action: Say, “I have not been trained on that yet. Can someone show me the safe way?”\n\n### **Situation 4**\n\nYou notice a road case blocking an emergency exit.\n\nBest action: Tell your lead immediately. Do not ignore blocked exits or fire lanes.\n\n### **Situation 5**\n\nYou see another worker doing something unsafe, but you are not their supervisor.\n\nBest action: Do not create conflict. Report the concern to your lead or the proper person.\n\n## **Simple Phrases for Speaking Up**\n\nNew stagehands do not need complicated language. Use clear, calm phrases:\n\n● “I am not sure that is safe.”\n ● “Can we check with the lead first?”\n ● “I have not been trained on that.”\n ● “There is a hazard over here.”\n ● “This path is blocked.”\n ● “That case looks unstable.”\n ● “Do you want me to pause and wait?”\n ● “Can you show me the safe way to do that?”\n ● “Who should I report this to?”\n\n## **Important Safety Note**\n\nThis lesson is safety-awareness training for entry-level stagehands. It is not legal advice, OSHA certification, OSHA 10/30 training, union training, employer-specific training, or equipment certification.\n\nThis lesson discusses general U.S. OSHA worker-safety concepts. Exact responsibilities may vary depending on employer, jurisdiction, work arrangement, venue policy, contract requirements, and site-specific procedures.\n\nFor this course, the beginner rule is simple:\n\nIf something seems unsafe, unclear, or outside your training, pause your own work, move to a safe position if needed, and ask the correct lead before continuing.\n\n## **Key Concept**\n\nSafety is a shared responsibility. Employers must provide a safe workplace, training, procedures, and required safety equipment according to applicable rules. Workers must follow safety rules, use PPE, report hazards, and ask questions when something is unclear or unsafe.\n\n## **Reflection Question**\n\nBefore moving on, ask yourself:\n\nIf I saw a hazard on a jobsite, would I know how to speak up clearly without creating confusion or conflict?\n\n## **Quick Knowledge Check**\n\n**Question:** What should a new stagehand do if they are asked to perform a task they have not been trained or authorized to do?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n They should pause and communicate clearly. A good response is: “I have not been trained on that yet. Can someone show me the safe way, or would you like me to help with something else?”\n\n## **Key Takeaway**\n\nNew stagehands have the right to a safe workplace, understandable safety training, required safety equipment according to applicable rules, and the ability to report hazards. They also have the responsibility to follow instructions, wear required PPE, report unsafe conditions, and ask questions when something is unclear or outside their training. Safety is not separate from professionalism. It is part of the job.\n\nLesson 2.3\n\n# **Module 2, Lesson 3: The Difference Between Hazard, Risk, and Unsafe Behavior**\n\n## **Lesson Purpose**\n\nThis lesson teaches new stagehands how to tell the difference between a hazard, a risk, and an unsafe behavior.\n\nThe goal is to help learners stop using the word “unsafe” too generally and start thinking more clearly about what they are seeing on a jobsite.\n\nA beginner does not need to become a safety professional on day one, but they do need to recognize when something could hurt someone, when the chance of injury is increasing, and when a person’s actions are making the situation worse.\n\n## **Learning Objective**\n\nBy the end of this lesson, the learner should be able to explain the difference between a hazard, a risk, and unsafe behavior, and identify basic examples of each in a live event work environment.\n\n## **Main Teaching Points**\n\nA hazard is something that could cause harm if it is not controlled.\n\nA risk is the chance that the hazard will actually cause harm and how serious the outcome could be.\n\nAn unsafe behavior is an action that increases danger.\n\nOSHA’s safety-management guidance emphasizes identifying and assessing hazards, investigating close calls and incidents, and considering both severity and likelihood when prioritizing corrective actions.\n\nFor stagehands, this matters because live event jobsites change quickly. A cable path may be safe one minute and become a trip hazard the next. A road case may be stable when parked but dangerous when pushed too fast down a ramp. A forklift may not be a problem when parked, but the risk increases when it starts moving through a crowded work area.\n\nThe professional stagehand learns to ask:\n\nWhat is the hazard?\n How serious is the risk?\n Is someone’s behavior making it worse?\n Who should I tell?\n\n## **Short Lesson Script**\n\nOn a live event jobsite, people often use the word “unsafe” to describe anything that feels dangerous.\n\nThat word is useful, but it is not specific enough by itself.\n\nTo become a safer stagehand, you need to understand the difference between a hazard, a risk, and unsafe behavior.\n\nA hazard is the thing that could cause harm.\n\nFor example, an open cable path across a walkway is a hazard. A wet floor is a hazard. A moving forklift is a hazard. A stacked road case that looks unstable is a hazard. Working under overhead rigging can be a hazard if the area is not properly controlled.\n\nThe hazard is the source of possible harm.\n\nRisk is the chance that the hazard will actually hurt someone, and how serious the outcome could be.\n\nFor example, a cable lying across a quiet backstage corner may be a hazard with lower risk if no one is walking there and it is marked or protected. That same cable across a dark load-out path where people are pushing cases becomes a much higher risk.\n\nThe cable is the hazard.\n The chance of someone tripping over it is the risk.\n\nUnsafe behavior is what a person does that increases the danger.\n\nRunning across the cable path is unsafe behavior.\n Pulling the cable without knowing what it is connected to is unsafe behavior.\n Dragging a road case through a crowded area without looking is unsafe behavior.\n Walking behind a forklift to save time is unsafe behavior.\n Standing under overhead work without direction is unsafe behavior.\n\nThis matters because not every hazard can disappear immediately. Live production involves gear, cable, cases, lifts, trucks, ramps, tools, temporary structures, and tight timelines. Some hazards are part of the environment, but they still need to be recognized and controlled.\n\nA professional stagehand does not ignore hazards.\n A professional stagehand does not create more risk.\n A professional stagehand does not copy unsafe behavior just because someone else is doing it.\n\nInstead, they slow down, look around, follow direction, use required PPE, stay out of restricted areas, and report problems to the correct lead.\n\nWhen you understand the difference between hazard, risk, and unsafe behavior, you become more useful. You can describe the problem clearly instead of saying, “That looks bad.”\n\nYou can say:\n\n“There is a cable across the main push path.”\n “The floor is wet near the dock door.”\n “That case stack looks unstable.”\n “There is a forklift moving through the path we are using.”\n “People are walking under overhead work.”\n “The load-out path is dark, and cases are moving through it.”\n\nClear communication helps the lead decide what needs to happen next.\n\n## **Simple Definitions**\n\n| **Term** | **Simple Meaning** | **Stagehand Example** |\n| --- | --- | --- |\n| Hazard | Something that could cause harm | A cable across a walkway |\n| Risk | How likely and how serious the harm could be | Someone may trip while pushing a case in low light |\n| Unsafe Behavior | An action that increases danger | Running across the cable path or pulling the cable without direction |\n\n## **Stagehand Examples**\n\n### **Example 1: Cable Path**\n\n**Hazard:** Cable lying across a walking or case path.\n **Risk:** Someone could trip, especially during load-out, in low light, or while pushing gear.\n **Unsafe behavior:** Running across the cable, dragging it without permission, or ignoring it when it is creating a clear hazard.\n\nBetter action: Report it to your lead or the correct department. If directed, help mark, protect, reroute, or clear the path.\n\n### **Example 2: Forklift Moving Nearby**\n\n**Hazard:** Forklift or powered equipment moving through the work area.\n **Risk:** A worker could be struck, pinned, or forced into another hazard.\n **Unsafe behavior:** Walking behind the forklift, cutting across its path, wearing headphones, or assuming the operator sees you.\n\nBetter action: Stay clear, keep your head up, follow site traffic rules, and wait for a safe path.\n\n### **Example 3: Stacked Road Cases**\n\n**Hazard:** Road cases stacked too high, unevenly, or on a slope.\n **Risk:** Cases could shift, roll, tip, or fall into workers or equipment.\n **Unsafe behavior:** Climbing on cases, leaning on unstable stacks, pushing a case without checking the stack, or stacking gear randomly.\n\nBetter action: Tell your lead if a stack looks unstable. Do not climb, move, or restack cases unless directed.\n\n### **Example 4: Wet Floor**\n\n**Hazard:** Water, spilled liquid, mud, or slick flooring.\n **Risk:** A worker could slip while carrying gear, pushing a case, or moving quickly.\n **Unsafe behavior:** Ignoring the wet spot, running through it, pushing cases over it without warning others, or failing to report it.\n\nBetter action: Report the wet area. If directed, help block it off, clean it up, or reroute traffic.\n\n### **Example 5: Working Under Rigging**\n\n**Hazard:** Overhead work, suspended loads, motors, truss, or rigging activity.\n **Risk:** Objects, tools, hardware, or flown equipment could fall or move unexpectedly.\n **Unsafe behavior:** Standing under suspended loads, walking into rigging zones without direction, touching rigging hardware, or distracting riggers.\n\nBetter action: Stay clear of overhead work unless specifically directed by the appropriate lead.\n\n### **Example 6: Poor Lighting During Load-Out**\n\n**Hazard:** Dark or poorly lit work area.\n **Risk:** Workers may miss cables, ramps, case wheels, dock edges, stairs, uneven flooring, or other people.\n **Unsafe behavior:** Moving fast through the dark, pushing cases without checking the path, or assuming the area is clear.\n\nBetter action: Slow down, use approved lighting if available, stay with the assigned path, and report lighting problems to your lead.\n\n## **How Risk Changes**\n\nRisk is not always the same. The same hazard can become more or less serious depending on the situation.\n\nA cable path becomes higher risk when:\n\n● The area is dark\n ● People are tired\n ● Cases are moving through the path\n ● Workers are carrying gear\n ● The cable is not marked or protected\n ● The area is crowded\n ● The crew is rushing during load-out\n\nA road case becomes higher risk when:\n\n● It is on a ramp\n ● It is heavy\n ● It has a bad caster\n ● The path is crowded\n ● The person pushing cannot see ahead\n ● The case is moving too fast\n ● Someone is standing downhill from it\n\nA forklift area becomes higher risk when:\n\n● Pedestrians are crossing the same path\n ● Visibility is poor\n ● Noise makes verbal warnings hard to hear\n ● Workers are distracted\n ● The operator has limited sightlines\n ● People assume they have been seen\n\n## **What Beginners Should Do When They Notice a Hazard**\n\nA new stagehand does not need to solve every hazard alone.\n\nIn many cases, the correct response is to report the issue and wait for direction.\n\nUse this simple process:\n\n- **Notice it**\n Look for anything that could hurt someone or damage equipment.\n\n- **Name it clearly**\n Say what the hazard is.\n\n- **Avoid making it worse**\n Do not move gear, touch cable, enter restricted areas, or start fixing things unless directed.\n\n- **Tell the correct person**\n Report the concern to your lead, department head, safety person, or proper contact.\n\n- **Follow direction**\n Once the lead gives instructions, help only in the way you are assigned.\n\n## **Simple Phrases for Reporting Hazards**\n\n● “There is a cable across the main push path.”\n ● “The floor is wet near the dock.”\n ● “That case stack looks unstable.”\n ● “There is a forklift moving through the area we are using.”\n ● “People are walking under overhead work.”\n ● “The load-out path is too dark to see clearly.”\n ● “This exit path is blocked.”\n ● “I am not sure this is safe. Who should I tell?”\n ● “Should we pause and check with the lead before moving this?”\n\n## **Common Beginner Mistakes**\n\n### **Mistake 1: Thinking a Hazard Is Only Dangerous After Someone Gets Hurt**\n\nA hazard matters before an injury happens. The goal is prevention.\n\n### **Mistake 2: Trying to Fix Every Hazard Alone**\n\nSome hazards can be corrected quickly, such as picking up trash from a clear walkway. But many stagehand hazards involve department gear, moving equipment, rigging zones, electrical systems, or production flow.\n\nBetter choice: Report first. Act only when directed.\n\n### **Mistake 3: Calling Everything Unsafe Without Explaining Why**\n\nA clear report is more useful than a vague complaint.\n\nInstead of saying:\n “This area is sketchy.”\n\nSay:\n “There is poor lighting near the ramp, and cases are moving through that path.”\n\n### **Mistake 4: Copying Unsafe Behavior**\n\nJust because someone else walks through a restricted area, skips PPE, runs with gear, or climbs on a road case does not mean it is acceptable.\n\nBetter choice: Follow your training, site rules, and lead instructions.\n\n### **Mistake 5: Becoming the Hazard**\n\nSometimes the unsafe condition is not the gear. It is the worker’s behavior.\n\nA stagehand can become the hazard by standing in a traffic path, blocking an exit, leaving tools on the floor, walking under overhead work, pushing cases too fast, or acting without direction.\n\n## **Practical Activity: Identify the Hazard, Risk, and Unsafe Behavior**\n\nReview each situation and identify the hazard, the risk, and the unsafe behavior.\n\n### **Scenario 1**\n\nA cable is stretched across a hallway during load-out. The hallway is dim, and workers are pushing cases through it.\n\n**Hazard:** Cable across the hallway.\n **Risk:** Someone could trip, fall, or lose control of a case.\n **Unsafe behavior:** Rushing through the hallway without addressing or reporting the cable path.\n\n### **Scenario 2**\n\nA forklift is backing near the dock while two workers walk behind it to save time.\n\n**Hazard:** Moving forklift.\n **Risk:** Workers could be struck or pinned.\n **Unsafe behavior:** Walking behind moving equipment.\n\n### **Scenario 3**\n\nThree road cases are stacked unevenly near a ramp, and someone leans against the stack.\n\n**Hazard:** Unstable stack of road cases.\n **Risk:** Cases could shift, fall, or roll.\n **Unsafe behavior:** Leaning on the stack instead of staying clear and reporting it.\n\n### **Scenario 4**\n\nThe crew is working outside, and rain has made the ground muddy near the stage entrance.\n\n**Hazard:** Wet, muddy, uneven ground.\n **Risk:** Slips, trips, falls, or stuck wheels.\n **Unsafe behavior:** Running through the area or pushing cases without checking footing.\n\n### **Scenario 5**\n\nRiggers are working overhead, and a new stagehand walks underneath to get a better look.\n\n**Hazard:** Overhead rigging work.\n **Risk:** Falling objects or moving suspended equipment.\n **Unsafe behavior:** Walking under overhead work without direction.\n\n## **Important Safety Note**\n\nThis lesson is safety-awareness training for entry-level stagehands. It does not replace employer-provided safety training, site-specific instruction, union training, equipment certification, or OSHA 10/30 training.\n\nDo not attempt to fix specialized hazards involving rigging, electrical systems, forklifts, lifts, stage machinery, temporary structures, or department equipment unless you are trained, authorized, and assigned.\n\nIf something seems unsafe, unclear, or outside your training, pause your own work, move to a safe position if needed, and ask the correct lead.\n\n## **Key Concept**\n\nA hazard is something that could cause harm. Risk is how likely and how serious that harm could be. Unsafe behavior is an action that increases the danger.\n\nA professional stagehand learns to recognize all three.\n\n## **Reflection Question**\n\nBefore moving on, ask yourself:\n\nCan I look at a jobsite situation and explain what the hazard is, what the risk is, and what behavior might make it worse?\n\n## **Quick Knowledge Check**\n\n**Question:** What is the difference between a hazard and a risk?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n A hazard is something that could cause harm. Risk is the chance that the hazard will actually cause harm and how serious the outcome could be.\n\n## **Key Takeaway**\n\nStagehands need to recognize hazards before someone gets hurt. Risk changes as conditions change. A cable, forklift, wet floor, unstable road case, overhead work area, or dark load-out path can all become more serious when visibility drops, workers rush, fatigue increases, traffic increases, or unsafe behavior is added. New stagehands should notice hazards, avoid making them worse, report them clearly, and follow direction from the correct lead.\n\nLesson 2.4\n\n# **Module 2, Lesson 4: Pause and Get a Lead: When to Ask Questions**\n\n## **Lesson Purpose**\n\nThis lesson teaches new stagehands when to pause, ask questions, and get a lead instead of guessing or continuing into a potentially unsafe situation.\n\nThe goal is to help learners understand that pausing their own work does not mean being difficult, lazy, or unwilling to work. It means knowing when a situation is unclear, unsafe, outside your training, or outside your authority.\n\nA professional stagehand does not keep going just to look confident. A professional stagehand knows when to pause, communicate, and get the right person involved.\n\n## **Learning Objective**\n\nBy the end of this lesson, the learner should be able to identify situations where they should pause their own work, ask for clarification, or get a lead before continuing.\n\n## **Main Teaching Points**\n\nLive event jobsites move quickly, but fast movement does not remove the need for safe decision-making.\n\nA new stagehand should pause and ask when:\n\n● The instruction is unclear\n ● The task seems unsafe\n ● The task involves equipment they have not been trained to use\n ● The task involves rigging, power, lifts, forklifts, stage machinery, or other specialized work\n ● Gear appears unstable, damaged, connected, or not ready to move\n ● A path is blocked, wet, dark, crowded, or crossed by moving equipment\n ● People are working overhead\n ● They do not know who has authority over the task\n ● They feel pressure to guess instead of confirm\n\nPausing your own work is not about refusing every task. It is about preventing avoidable mistakes before they become injuries, damage, delays, or confusion.\n\nFor beginners, the safest rule is simple:\n\nWhen you are unsure, pause your own work, move to a safe position if needed, and ask the correct lead.\n\n## **Short Lesson Script**\n\nOn a live event jobsite, things can move fast.\n\nCases are rolling. Trucks are unloading. Forklifts are moving. Cables are being run. Departments are building their systems. Leads are giving instructions. Everyone is trying to get the show ready or get the gear packed and loaded out.\n\nIn that environment, it can feel uncomfortable to pause and ask a question.\n\nYou may think asking questions makes you look inexperienced.\n\nBut guessing is much worse.\n\nA new stagehand who guesses can move the wrong case, pull the wrong cable, walk into a restricted area, block a fire lane, interrupt a department, damage equipment, or put someone in danger.\n\nA pause-and-ask mindset means you understand when to stop your own action before continuing.\n\nIt does not mean you stop the whole show.\n It does not mean you argue with your lead.\n It does not mean you refuse to help.\n It means you recognize that something needs clarification before you act.\n\nFor example, if someone tells you to move a road case but you do not know where it goes, pause and ask.\n\nIf you are told to pull a cable but you do not know what it is connected to, pause and ask.\n\nIf a forklift is moving through the same path you were told to use, pause and wait for safe direction.\n\nIf people are working overhead, do not walk underneath just because it is the fastest route.\n\nIf someone asks you to operate a lift, forklift, motor, power equipment, stage machinery, or anything you have not been trained and authorized to use, pause and tell them you have not been trained on it.\n\nThat is not weakness. That is professionalism.\n\nThe best crew members do not want beginners pretending. They want beginners who communicate clearly, follow direction, and know when to get help.\n\nA simple question can prevent a serious problem.\n\nYou can say:\n\n“Can you clarify where this goes?”\n “I have not been trained on that.”\n “Should we check with the lead first?”\n “This path does not look clear.”\n “Is this safe to move?”\n “Who should I report this to?”\n\nThose phrases show awareness.\n\nAs a new stagehand, your job is not to solve every problem alone. Your job is to notice when something is unclear or unsafe, pause before making it worse, and involve the right person.\n\n## **What “Pause and Get a Lead” Means**\n\n| **It Does Mean** | **It Does Not Mean** |\n| --- | --- |\n| Pausing your own action when something is unclear | Refusing every task |\n| Asking for clarification | Arguing with leads |\n| Reporting hazards | Creating drama |\n| Admitting when you are not trained | Acting helpless |\n| Waiting for safe direction | Wandering away |\n| Protecting people, gear, and the venue | Slowing the job down on purpose |\n\n## **When to Pause and Ask**\n\n### **1. When You Do Not Understand the Instruction**\n\nIf you only understood part of the instruction, do not guess.\n\nExample:\n A lead says, “Take those to stage left and stack them by the line array cases.”\n\nIf you do not know where stage left is or what the line array cases are, ask.\n\nBetter response:\n “Can you point me to stage left and show me which cases you mean?”\n\n### **2. When You Are Not Trained or Authorized**\n\nDo not operate specialized equipment just because someone nearby asks you to.\n\nThis includes:\n\n● Forklifts\n ● Aerial lifts\n ● Rigging motors\n ● Power distribution equipment\n ● Stage automation\n ● Fly systems\n ● Chain motors\n ● Hoists\n ● Pyrotechnics\n ● Lasers\n ● Heavy machinery\n ● Any equipment requiring specific authorization\n\nBetter response:\n “I have not been trained on that equipment. Do you want me to get someone who is authorized?”\n\n### **3. When Gear May Still Be Connected**\n\nDo not pull, coil, unplug, or move cable unless directed by the correct department.\n\nExample:\n A cable may still be connected to power, audio, lighting, video, data, or control systems.\n\nBetter response:\n “Do you know if this has been cleared by the department?”\n\n### **4. When the Path Is Not Safe**\n\nPause before moving gear through a path that is blocked, crowded, wet, dark, or crossed by moving equipment.\n\nExamples:\n\n● Forklift path\n ● Truck ramp\n ● Dock plate\n ● Wet floor\n ● Dark hallway\n ● Cable crossing\n ● Fire lane\n ● Emergency exit\n ● Crowded backstage area\n\nBetter response:\n “This path is blocked. Do you want us to wait or use another route?”\n\n### **5. When People Are Working Overhead**\n\nNever assume it is safe to walk under rigging, truss, motors, tools, lifts, or suspended equipment.\n\nBetter response:\n “Is this area clear to walk through, or should we wait?”\n\n### **6. When a Load Looks Unstable**\n\nPause if a road case, cart, stack, scenic piece, deck, truss, or equipment pile looks like it could shift, fall, roll, or tip.\n\nBetter response:\n “That stack looks unstable. Can we get a lead to check it before moving anything?”\n\n### **7. When You Are Being Asked to Rush Unsafely**\n\nWorking efficiently is good. Rushing blindly is not.\n\nPause and ask if speed is causing people to skip communication, ignore hazards, run with gear, overload carts, block exits, or move equipment without control.\n\nBetter response:\n “I can move quickly, but I want to make sure we have a clear path first.”\n\n## **The Beginner Pause-and-Ask Process**\n\nUse this simple process:\n\n### **Step 1: Pause**\n\nPause your own action before making the situation worse.\n\n### **Step 2: Step Out of the Hazard**\n\nMove yourself to a safer position if needed. Do not block traffic, stand under overhead work, or remain in the path of moving equipment.\n\n### **Step 3: Communicate Clearly**\n\nSay what you see or what you do not understand.\n\nExample:\n “I am not sure this cable is cleared to move.”\n\n### **Step 4: Get the Correct Lead**\n\nAsk your crew lead, department head, safety person, or assigned point of contact.\n\n### **Step 5: Follow Direction**\n\nOnce the correct person gives instruction, follow it carefully.\n\n## **Simple Phrases New Stagehands Can Use**\n\n● “Can you clarify that before I start?”\n ● “Where exactly do you want this case?”\n ● “I have not been trained on that.”\n ● “I am not sure this is safe to move.”\n ● “This path is blocked.”\n ● “There is a forklift moving through this area.”\n ● “Do you want me to wait for the department?”\n ● “Should we get a lead before we move this?”\n ● “Is this area clear, or is overhead work still happening?”\n ● “Can someone show me the safe way to do that?”\n\n## **Example Beginner Situations**\n\n### **Situation 1: The Unclear Case Move**\n\nA crew member points to a group of road cases and says, “Take those over there.”\n\nYou are not sure which cases they mean or where “over there” is.\n\nBest action: Pause and ask for clarification.\n\nBetter response:\n “Which cases do you mean, and where exactly should they go?”\n\n### **Situation 2: The Cable Pull**\n\nDuring load-out, someone tells you to pull a cable across the stage. You do not know what it is connected to.\n\nBest action: Pause and confirm with the correct department or lead.\n\nBetter response:\n “Has this cable been cleared to pull?”\n\n### **Situation 3: The Forklift Path**\n\nYou are pushing a case and see a forklift moving through the same path.\n\nBest action: Pause, stay clear, and wait for safe direction.\n\nBetter response:\n “Forklift is moving through the path. Should we hold here?”\n\n### **Situation 4: The Untrained Equipment Request**\n\nSomeone asks you to hop on a lift and move it a few feet.\n\nYou have not been trained or authorized to operate it.\n\nBest action: Do not operate it.\n\nBetter response:\n “I have not been trained or authorized on that lift. We need someone qualified.”\n\n### **Situation 5: The Overhead Work Area**\n\nYou see riggers working overhead, but the fastest way across the room is underneath them.\n\nBest action: Stay clear and ask if the area is open.\n\nBetter response:\n “Is this area clear to pass through, or should we go around?”\n\n### **Situation 6: The Unstable Stack**\n\nYou notice stacked road cases leaning slightly near a ramp.\n\nBest action: Do not touch the stack unless directed. Report it.\n\nBetter response:\n “That case stack looks unstable near the ramp. Can someone check it before we move anything?”\n\n## **What Not to Do**\n\nA new stagehand should avoid:\n\n● Pretending to understand instructions\n ● Moving gear without knowing where it goes\n ● Pulling cable without clearance\n ● Walking under overhead work\n ● Operating equipment without training\n ● Entering restricted areas without direction\n ● Ignoring blocked exits or fire lanes\n ● Running with gear\n ● Copying unsafe behavior\n ● Taking direction from random people instead of the correct lead\n\n## **Important Safety Note**\n\nThis lesson teaches a beginner safety mindset. It is not legal advice, OSHA 10/30 training, equipment certification, union training, or employer-specific safety training.\n\nOSHA recommends bringing unsafe or unhealthful working conditions to the employer’s attention when possible. OSHA also explains that the legal right to refuse dangerous work has specific conditions, including a good-faith belief of imminent danger, a reasonable risk of death or serious injury, and not enough time to correct the issue through regular enforcement channels.\n\nFor this course, the practical beginner rule is:\n\nIf something seems unsafe, unclear, or outside your training, pause your own work, move to a safe position if needed, and ask the correct lead before continuing.\n\n## **Key Concept**\n\nA pause-and-ask mindset means knowing when to pause before acting. It is not about being difficult. It is about preventing injuries, confusion, damage, and unsafe shortcuts.\n\n## **Reflection Question**\n\nBefore moving on, ask yourself:\n\nWould I rather ask a question and look new for ten seconds, or guess and create a serious problem for the crew?\n\n## **Quick Knowledge Check**\n\n**Question:** What should a new stagehand do if they are unsure whether a task is safe or they have not been trained to do it?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n They should pause their own work, avoid making the situation worse, communicate clearly, and ask the correct lead or department head for direction before continuing.\n\n## **Key Takeaway**\n\nNew stagehands should never pretend, guess, or rush into unsafe work. A professional stagehand knows when to pause, ask questions, and get a lead. Pausing to clarify is not weakness. It is part of working safely, protecting the crew, and becoming someone people trust on the job.\n\nLesson 2.5\n\n# **Module 2, Lesson 5: Incident Reporting and Near Misses**\n\n## **Lesson Purpose**\n\nThis lesson teaches new stagehands why incidents, injuries, hazards, and near misses should be reported instead of ignored.\n\nThe goal is to help learners understand that reporting is not about getting someone in trouble. It is about preventing the same situation from hurting someone later.\n\nA professional stagehand does not hide mistakes, ignore close calls, or pretend nothing happened. A professional stagehand communicates clearly so the crew can correct hazards before they become serious.\n\n## **Learning Objective**\n\nBy the end of this lesson, the learner should be able to explain what an incident and near miss are, why they matter, and what a new stagehand should do after seeing or being involved in one.\n\n## **Main Teaching Points**\n\nAn incident is an unplanned event that causes injury, illness, damage, or disruption.\n\nA near miss is an event where someone could have been hurt, but was not.\n\nNear misses matter because they are warning signs. OSHA’s safety-management guidance includes investigating injuries, illnesses, incidents, and close calls or near misses to identify underlying hazards, causes, and safety program shortcomings.\n\nIf a road case almost tips over, that matters.\n If a forklift almost backs into someone, that matters.\n If a worker almost trips over a cable path, that matters.\n If a tool falls from height but misses everyone, that matters.\n If a ramp shifts while cases are moving over it, that matters.\n\nNo injury happened, but the hazard was real.\n\nFor new stagehands, the rule is simple:\n\nIf someone gets hurt, something gets damaged, or something almost causes harm, report it to the correct lead right away.\n\n## **Short Lesson Script**\n\nOn a live event jobsite, not every safety problem looks dramatic.\n\nSometimes a serious warning sign happens quickly, and then the crew keeps moving.\n\nA road case almost rolls off a ramp, but someone catches it.\n A worker slips on a wet floor but does not fall.\n A forklift horn sounds because someone stepped into its path.\n A cable gets pulled before it is cleared, but luckily nothing breaks.\n A tool drops from a lift but does not hit anyone.\n A case stack shifts but does not fall.\n\nIt can be tempting to say, “No one got hurt, so we are fine.”\n\nBut that is the wrong lesson.\n\nA near miss is important because it shows that the conditions were present for someone to get hurt. The only difference between a near miss and an injury may be timing, luck, or a few inches.\n\nProfessional crews do not ignore near misses. They learn from them.\n\nIncident reporting and near-miss reporting help leads, employers, venues, and crews identify problems before they happen again. Maybe a path needs to be cleared. Maybe a ramp needs to be secured. Maybe a cable path needs to be marked. Maybe a case stack needs to be rebuilt. Maybe the crew needs better lighting, better communication, or a slower pace in a certain area.\n\nReporting does not mean you are blaming someone.\n\nIt means you are giving the crew important information.\n\nAs a new stagehand, you may feel nervous about reporting something. You may worry that people will think you are complaining, overreacting, or trying to get someone in trouble.\n\nBut safety communication is part of the job.\n\nIf someone is seriously hurt or there is immediate danger, follow the site emergency procedure first, get help, and do not delay emergency response just to complete a report.\n\nIf you are injured, report it.\n If someone else is injured, report it.\n If gear is damaged, report it.\n If you see a serious hazard, report it.\n If something almost causes an injury, report it.\n\nDo not hide it.\n Do not walk away from it.\n Do not assume someone else saw it.\n Do not wait until the end of the call if the hazard is active.\n\nTell your lead, department head, supervisor, safety person, or assigned point of contact.\n\nUse calm, clear language.\n\nYou can say:\n\n“Someone almost tripped over that cable path.”\n “That case almost tipped on the ramp.”\n “The floor is wet near the dock.”\n “A tool fell from above.”\n “That forklift came close to the crew path.”\n “I cut my hand and need to report it.”\n “I saw a near miss and want to let someone know.”\n\nA safe crew does not depend on luck. A safe crew depends on awareness, communication, and correction.\n\n## **Simple Definitions**\n\n| **Term** | **Meaning** | **Stagehand Example** |\n| --- | --- | --- |\n| Hazard | Something that could cause harm | Wet floor near the dock |\n| Incident | Something happened that caused injury, damage, illness, or disruption | A worker slips and injures their wrist |\n| Near Miss | Something almost caused harm, but did not | A worker slips on the wet floor but catches themselves |\n| Report | Telling the correct person what happened | Informing your lead that the floor is wet and someone almost fell |\n\n## **Incident vs. Near Miss**\n\n### **Incident**\n\nAn incident means something actually happened.\n\nExamples:\n\n● A worker gets hurt\n ● A road case hits a wall or damages gear\n ● A cable is pulled and damages equipment\n ● A person falls\n ● A tool drops and hits someone or something\n ● A vehicle or forklift strikes an object\n ● A fire lane or emergency exit is blocked and causes a response issue\n\n### **Near Miss**\n\nA near miss means something almost happened.\n\nExamples:\n\n● A worker almost trips over cable\n ● A case almost tips over\n ● A forklift almost backs into a crew member\n ● A ramp shifts while a case is moving\n ● A tool falls but misses everyone\n ● Someone nearly walks under overhead work\n ● A case rolls away but is stopped before hitting anyone\n\n## **Why Near Misses Matter**\n\nNear misses are valuable because they show the crew where danger is building.\n\nA near miss can reveal:\n\n● A blocked path\n ● Poor lighting\n ● Bad communication\n ● Missing signage or barricades\n ● Unstable gear\n ● Unsafe speed\n ● Fatigue\n ● Improper stacking\n ● Confusing directions\n ● Crowded work zones\n ● Forklift and pedestrian traffic conflicts\n ● Cable paths that need to be marked or protected\n\nIf the crew ignores the near miss, the same condition may cause a real injury later.\n\n## **What New Stagehands Should Report**\n\nA new stagehand should report:\n\n● Any injury, even if it seems minor\n ● Any illness or medical concern that happens at work\n ● Any gear damage\n ● Any vehicle, forklift, cart, case, or pallet jack contact incident\n ● Any fall, slip, trip, or dropped object\n ● Any blocked exit, fire lane, ramp, dock, or pathway\n ● Any unstable road case stack or rolling gear problem\n ● Any serious cable trip hazard\n ● Any unsafe overhead work concern\n ● Any task they were asked to do but were not trained or authorized for\n ● Any near miss where someone could have been hurt\n\nOSHA’s safety-management guidance says workers should know how to report hazards, injuries, illnesses, and close calls or near misses, and know what to do in an emergency.\n\n## **What to Do After an Incident or Near Miss**\n\n### **Step 1: Respond to Immediate Danger First**\n\nIf someone is seriously hurt or there is immediate danger, follow the site emergency procedure first, get help, and do not delay emergency response just to complete a report.\n\nDo not disturb the area of a serious incident unless necessary to prevent further harm or follow emergency instructions.\n\n### **Step 2: Make Sure You Are Safe**\n\nDo not step into more danger.\n\nIf the area has moving equipment, overhead work, electrical hazards, unstable gear, or traffic, move to a safe position first.\n\n### **Step 3: Help Prevent Immediate Harm**\n\nIf you can safely warn others, do so.\n\nExamples:\n\n● “Watch the wet floor.”\n ● “Hold up, forklift moving.”\n ● “Do not use that path yet.”\n ● “Stay clear of that stack.”\n\nDo not attempt specialized fixes unless trained and assigned.\n\n### **Step 4: Tell the Correct Person**\n\nReport the issue to your lead, department head, supervisor, safety person, or assigned point of contact.\n\n### **Step 5: Be Clear and Factual**\n\nSay what happened, where it happened, who was involved if known, and whether anyone was hurt.\n\nGood report:\n\n“Near stage left, a worker almost tripped over an unmarked cable path while pushing a case. No one is hurt, but that path is still active.”\n\nPoor report:\n\n“Everything over there is sketchy.”\n\n### **Step 6: Follow Site Procedure**\n\nYour employer or venue may have a specific reporting process. Follow it.\n\nYou may be asked to fill out a form, give a statement, speak to a supervisor, or identify the location of the hazard.\n\n### **Step 7: Do Not Hide or Minimize Injuries**\n\nIf you are injured, report it.\n\nDo not say “I’m fine” just because you want to keep working. Some injuries get worse after adrenaline wears off.\n\n## **What to Include in a Report**\n\n| **Report Detail** | **Example** |\n| --- | --- |\n| What happened | “A road case almost tipped.” |\n| Where it happened | “At the dock ramp.” |\n| When it happened | “During load-out, about ten minutes ago.” |\n| Who was involved | “Two crew members were pushing the case.” |\n| Whether anyone was hurt | “No one was injured.” |\n| What hazard remains | “The ramp still looks uneven.” |\n| What help is needed | “Can a lead check it before we move more cases?” |\n\n## **Simple Reporting Phrases**\n\nNew stagehands can use simple language:\n\n● “I need to report an injury.”\n ● “There was a near miss by the dock.”\n ● “Someone almost tripped over that cable.”\n ● “A case almost tipped on the ramp.”\n ● “This path is blocked and people are pushing cases through it.”\n ● “The floor is wet near the load-out path.”\n ● “A tool fell from overhead.”\n ● “That stack looks unstable.”\n ● “Nobody was hurt, but it could have been serious.”\n ● “Who should I report this to?”\n\n## **Example Beginner Situations**\n\n### **Situation 1: The Almost Trip**\n\nDuring load-out, a crew member catches their foot on an unmarked cable but does not fall.\n\nBest action: Report it as a near miss.\n\nBetter response:\n “Someone almost tripped over the cable path near stage right. Can we get that checked or marked?”\n\n### **Situation 2: The Small Cut**\n\nYou cut your hand while moving a case. It does not seem serious.\n\nBest action: Report it according to your employer’s process and get first aid if needed.\n\nBetter response:\n “I cut my hand while moving that case. I need to report it and clean it up.”\n\n### **Situation 3: The Rolling Case**\n\nA road case starts rolling down a slight ramp but is stopped before hitting anyone.\n\nBest action: Report it as a near miss.\n\nBetter response:\n “That case started rolling on its own near the ramp. No one was hurt, but we should check the brakes or the parking spot.”\n\n### **Situation 4: The Dropped Tool**\n\nA tool falls from height and lands near the crew, but nobody is hit.\n\nBest action: Stay clear and report it immediately.\n\nBetter response:\n “A tool fell from overhead near the work area. Nobody was hit, but we need to report it.”\n\n### **Situation 5: The Damaged Gear**\n\nA case is pushed into a doorway and damages the doorframe.\n\nBest action: Report the damage. Do not hide it.\n\nBetter response:\n “The case hit the doorway and damaged the frame. Who should I report that to?”\n\n### **Situation 6: The Forklift Close Call**\n\nA forklift backs near a group of stagehands, and someone has to jump out of the way.\n\nBest action: Report the close call to your lead.\n\nBetter response:\n “There was a close call with the forklift near the dock path. No one was hit, but the crew path and forklift path are crossing.”\n\n## **Common Beginner Mistakes**\n\n### **Mistake 1: Thinking “No Injury” Means “No Problem”**\n\nA near miss still matters. It shows that a hazard exists.\n\n### **Mistake 2: Hiding Small Injuries**\n\nA small cut, strain, slip, or hit can become more serious later. Report injuries according to your employer’s process.\n\n### **Mistake 3: Waiting Too Long**\n\nA hazard should be reported while it still matters, not after the call is over and everyone has left.\n\n### **Mistake 4: Blaming Instead of Reporting**\n\nThe goal is to communicate facts and prevent future harm.\n\nInstead of saying:\n “Those guys are being stupid with the cases.”\n\nSay:\n “A case almost tipped near the ramp while the crew was moving quickly. Can a lead check that area?”\n\n### **Mistake 5: Assuming Someone Else Reported It**\n\nIf you saw it and it matters, tell the correct person.\n\n### **Mistake 6: Trying to Fix Specialized Problems Alone**\n\nDo not fix rigging, electrical, lift, forklift, staging, automation, or department-specific hazards unless trained, authorized, and assigned.\n\n## **Reporting Is Not Snitching**\n\nSome new workers worry that reporting makes them look difficult.\n\nThat mindset is dangerous.\n\nReporting a hazard, incident, injury, or near miss is not snitching. It is part of jobsite safety.\n\nA good report helps the crew:\n\n● Prevent injuries\n ● Protect gear\n ● Keep paths clear\n ● Improve communication\n ● Correct hazards\n ● Document what happened\n ● Learn from close calls\n ● Avoid repeating the same mistake\n\nProfessional crews should use honest safety communication to improve the work.\n\n## **Important Safety Note**\n\nThis lesson is safety-awareness training for entry-level stagehands. It does not replace employer-provided safety training, site-specific reporting procedures, union training, legal advice, equipment certification, or OSHA 10/30 training.\n\nIn the United States, OSHA requires covered employers to report work-related fatalities to OSHA within 8 hours and work-related inpatient hospitalizations, amputations, or loss of an eye within 24 hours. That responsibility belongs to the employer, not the entry-level stagehand.\n\nFor the learner, the practical rule is:\n\nIf someone is seriously hurt or there is immediate danger, follow the site emergency procedure first. Then report injuries, damage, hazards, and near misses to the correct lead or supervisor as soon as possible.\n\n## **Key Concept**\n\nNear misses are warnings. If something almost caused an injury, damage, or serious hazard, it should be reported so the crew can correct the problem before someone gets hurt.\n\n## **Reflection Question**\n\nBefore moving on, ask yourself:\n\nIf I saw a close call where no one got hurt, would I ignore it, or would I report it so the crew could prevent it from happening again?\n\n## **Quick Knowledge Check**\n\n**Question:** What is a near miss?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n A near miss is an event where someone could have been hurt, but was not. It should still be reported because it can reveal a hazard that needs to be corrected.\n\n## **Key Takeaway**\n\nIncident reporting and near-miss reporting are part of professional stagehand work. New stagehands should report injuries, hazards, damage, and close calls clearly and quickly to the correct lead or supervisor. Immediate danger and serious injuries come first: follow the site emergency procedure, get help, and do not delay emergency response just to complete a report. Reporting is not about blame. It is about preventing the next incident.\n\nLesson 2.6\n\n# **Module 2, Lesson 6: Why You Should Never Pretend You Know Something**\n\n## **Lesson Purpose**\n\nThis lesson teaches new stagehands why pretending to understand a task, tool, piece of gear, or safety instruction can create serious problems on a live event jobsite.\n\nThe goal is to help learners understand that honesty is a safety skill.\n\nA new stagehand is not expected to know everything. They are expected to listen, ask questions, admit when they are unsure, and avoid doing work they have not been trained or authorized to do.\n\n## **Learning Objective**\n\nBy the end of this lesson, the learner should be able to explain why pretending to know something is unsafe and describe what they should say when they do not understand a task, instruction, or piece of equipment.\n\n## **Main Teaching Points**\n\nNew stagehands may feel pressure to act confident. They may worry that asking questions makes them look inexperienced. They may want to prove they belong on the crew.\n\nBut pretending to know something can be dangerous.\n\nOn a live event jobsite, one wrong assumption can lead to injury, damaged gear, blocked workflows, delayed departments, or unsafe conditions.\n\nA stagehand should never pretend to understand:\n\n● Where gear is supposed to go\n ● Whether a cable is safe to move\n ● How to operate equipment\n ● Whether a path is clear\n ● What department owns a case or tool\n ● How to lift, push, stack, or secure something safely\n ● Whether they are authorized to perform a task\n ● What a lead’s instruction means\n\nOSHA states that employers are responsible for providing safety training in a language and vocabulary workers can understand, and OSHA’s safety management guidance says training should give workers knowledge and skills needed to work safely and avoid creating hazards.\n\nFor a beginner stagehand, that means this:\n\nIf you do not know, say you do not know.\n\nThat is not weakness. That is professionalism.\n\n## **Short Lesson Script**\n\nWhen you are new to stagehand work, you may feel pressure to prove yourself.\n\nYou might hear unfamiliar words.\n You might see gear you have never touched.\n You might be surrounded by experienced crew members who move quickly and seem to know exactly what they are doing.\n\nIn that environment, it can be tempting to nod your head and act like you understand.\n\nBut pretending is one of the fastest ways to create a problem.\n\nIf a lead tells you to take a case to stage left and you do not know where stage left is, do not guess.\n\nIf someone tells you to pull a cable and you do not know whether it is still connected, do not pull it.\n\nIf a crew member asks you to operate a lift, forklift, chain motor, power equipment, fly system, or any specialized equipment you have not been trained on, do not pretend you know how.\n\nIf you do not understand the instruction, stop and ask.\n\nIn live production, the wrong move can affect more than just you. Moving the wrong case can slow down a department. Pulling the wrong cable can damage equipment or interrupt a system. Walking into the wrong area can put you under overhead work. Trying to operate unfamiliar equipment can put people at serious risk.\n\nThe best new stagehands are honest.\n\nThey say:\n\n“I have not been trained on that.”\n “Can you show me the safe way?”\n “Can you clarify where this goes?”\n “I do not know that term yet.”\n “Is this cable cleared to move?”\n “Who should I ask before touching this?”\n\nThose phrases do not make you look weak. They make you look aware.\n\nCrew leads would rather answer a simple question than deal with an injury, damaged gear, or a task done wrong.\n\nThere is nothing wrong with being new. Every experienced stagehand started somewhere. What matters is whether you are safe, coachable, and honest about what you do and do not know.\n\nA dangerous beginner is not someone who lacks experience.\n\nA dangerous beginner is someone who hides their lack of experience and acts without direction.\n\nNever pretend. Ask, learn, and then do the task correctly.\n\n## **Why Pretending Is Dangerous**\n\n| **Pretending Sounds Like** | **What Can Go Wrong** | **Better Response** |\n| --- | --- | --- |\n| “Yeah, I got it,” when you do not understand | Gear may go to the wrong place | “Can you clarify where this should go?” |\n| “I know how to use that,” when you are not trained | You may injure yourself or others | “I have not been trained on that equipment.” |\n| “I think this cable is fine to pull” | You may damage active equipment | “Has this cable been cleared?” |\n| “I know where stage left is,” when you do not | You may slow down the department | “Can you point me toward stage left?” |\n| “I can move this alone” | You may lose control or strain yourself | “Do you want a team lift on this?” |\n| “I assume this path is clear” | You may hit someone or block traffic | “Let me check the path first.” |\n\n## **What New Stagehands Are Not Expected to Know Yet**\n\nA beginner is not expected to know every:\n\n● Production term\n ● Department role\n ● Cable type\n ● Road case label\n ● Stage direction\n ● Tool name\n ● Venue layout\n ● Rigging term\n ● Lighting fixture\n ● Audio component\n ● Video system\n ● Truck pack method\n ● Show-specific procedure\n\nThat is normal.\n\nWhat beginners are expected to do is:\n\n● Listen carefully\n ● Ask questions\n ● Follow direction\n ● Stay honest\n ● Avoid guessing\n ● Admit when they are not trained\n ● Report unsafe conditions\n ● Learn from correction\n ● Improve throughout the call\n\n## **What You Should Never Fake**\n\n### **1. Do Not Fake Understanding an Instruction**\n\nIf a lead gives you an instruction and you only understand part of it, ask before acting.\n\nExample:\n\nA lead says, “Take these to stage right and leave them by the dimmer beach.”\n\nYou do not know where stage right is or what “dimmer beach” means.\n\nDo not guess.\n\nBetter response:\n “Can you point me to stage right and show me where you want these landed?”\n\n### **2. Do Not Fake Equipment Training**\n\nNever operate equipment you have not been trained and authorized to use.\n\nThis includes:\n\n● Forklifts\n ● Aerial lifts\n ● Chain motors\n ● Rigging equipment\n ● Power distribution equipment\n ● Stage automation\n ● Fly systems\n ● Pyrotechnics\n ● Lasers\n ● Specialized tools\n ● Any equipment requiring employer authorization\n\nOSHA’s safety training guidance says additional or specialized training may be needed when work involves unique hazards.\n\nBetter response:\n “I have not been trained or authorized on that equipment.”\n\n### **3. Do Not Fake Cable Knowledge**\n\nCables may be connected to audio, lighting, video, power, data, control systems, or other department gear.\n\nDo not pull, unplug, coil, reroute, or move cable unless directed by the correct person.\n\nBetter response:\n “Has this cable been cleared to move?”\n\n### **4. Do Not Fake Department Knowledge**\n\nIf you do not know which department owns a case, tool, cable, truss, scenic piece, or piece of equipment, ask.\n\nBetter response:\n “Do you know which department this belongs to?”\n\n### **5. Do Not Fake Physical Ability**\n\nIf something is too heavy, awkward, unstable, or hard to control, say so before someone gets hurt.\n\nBetter response:\n “This feels like a team lift. Can we get another hand?”\n\n### **6. Do Not Fake Safety Confidence**\n\nIf something feels unsafe, unclear, or outside your training, stop and ask.\n\nBetter response:\n “I am not sure this is safe. Can we check with the lead?”\n\n## **Honest Phrases That Make You More Professional**\n\nNew stagehands should get comfortable using these phrases:\n\n● “I have not been trained on that.”\n ● “Can you show me the safe way?”\n ● “Can you clarify the instruction?”\n ● “Where exactly do you want this?”\n ● “Which case are you referring to?”\n ● “Is this cable cleared to move?”\n ● “Who should I report to for this task?”\n ● “I do not know that term yet.”\n ● “I want to make sure I do this correctly.”\n ● “Should we get a lead before moving this?”\n ● “This looks like it may need more people.”\n ● “I am not comfortable guessing on this.”\n\nThese phrases show maturity, not weakness.\n\n## **Example Beginner Situations**\n\n### **Situation 1: You Do Not Know the Stage Direction**\n\nA lead says, “Take those cases to stage left.”\n\nYou do not know which side is stage left.\n\nBest action: Ask immediately.\n\nBetter response:\n “Can you point me to stage left so I land these in the right place?”\n\n### **Situation 2: You Are Asked to Pull Cable**\n\nSomeone says, “Grab that cable and pull it back.”\n\nYou do not know what it is connected to.\n\nBest action: Do not pull it until it is cleared.\n\nBetter response:\n “Has this cable been cleared by the department?”\n\n### **Situation 3: You Are Asked to Use Equipment**\n\nA crew member says, “Move that lift over here real quick.”\n\nYou have not been trained or authorized to operate the lift.\n\nBest action: Do not operate it.\n\nBetter response:\n “I have not been trained or authorized on that lift. We need someone qualified.”\n\n### **Situation 4: You Do Not Recognize a Case Label**\n\nYou are told to move “the backline cases,” but you do not know which ones those are.\n\nBest action: Ask for identification.\n\nBetter response:\n “Can you show me which cases are backline?”\n\n### **Situation 5: You Are Unsure About a Lift**\n\nA road case feels too heavy to move safely by yourself.\n\nBest action: Ask for help before you strain yourself or lose control of the case.\n\nBetter response:\n “This feels like a two-person push. Can I get another hand?”\n\n### **Situation 6: You Missed Part of the Instruction**\n\nA lead gives fast instructions in a loud arena, and you only hear half of what was said.\n\nBest action: Ask them to repeat or clarify.\n\nBetter response:\n “I missed the last part. Can you repeat where this needs to go?”\n\n## **Confidence vs. False Confidence**\n\n| **Real Confidence** | **False Confidence** |\n| --- | --- |\n| Asking questions when needed | Pretending to understand |\n| Admitting what you have not been trained on | Acting like you know everything |\n| Following instructions carefully | Improvising without permission |\n| Learning from correction | Getting defensive |\n| Moving with awareness | Rushing to look useful |\n| Knowing your limits | Taking unsafe shortcuts |\n\nReal confidence is not pretending to know everything.\n\nReal confidence is being honest enough to learn safely.\n\n## **Common Beginner Mistakes**\n\n### **Mistake 1: Nodding Without Understanding**\n\nMany new stagehands nod because they do not want to slow down the lead.\n\nBetter choice: Ask a quick clarifying question.\n\n### **Mistake 2: Saying Yes to Every Task**\n\nBeing helpful does not mean saying yes to unsafe or unauthorized work.\n\nBetter choice: Be honest about your training and ask for direction.\n\n### **Mistake 3: Copying Experienced Workers**\n\nExperienced workers may use shortcuts, terms, or movements you do not understand yet.\n\nBetter choice: Follow your lead’s instruction and your own training level.\n\n### **Mistake 4: Touching Gear to Look Busy**\n\nMoving random gear can create problems.\n\nBetter choice: Check in with your lead before moving equipment.\n\n### **Mistake 5: Being Embarrassed to Ask**\n\nEveryone starts as a beginner.\n\nBetter choice: Ask once, learn it, and apply it correctly next time.\n\n## **The “Ask Before Acting” Rule**\n\nUse this rule when you are unsure:\n\nBefore touching, moving, pulling, plugging in, unplugging, operating, lifting, stacking, opening, closing, climbing, entering, or crossing anything you do not understand, ask.\n\nThat one habit can prevent many beginner mistakes.\n\n## **What Leads Usually Want From New Stagehands**\n\nMost leads do not expect beginners to know everything.\n\nThey usually want beginners who:\n\n● Show up prepared\n ● Listen carefully\n ● Follow the chain of command\n ● Ask when unsure\n ● Do not touch gear without direction\n ● Admit training limits\n ● Work safely\n ● Stay calm when corrected\n ● Improve throughout the call\n\nA beginner who asks good questions is easier to train than a beginner who pretends.\n\n## **Practical Activity: Better Response Practice**\n\nRead each situation and choose a better response.\n\n### **Scenario 1**\n\nYou are told to take cases to “upstage right,” but you do not know where that is.\n\nPoor response:\n “Got it,” then guess.\n\nBetter response:\n “Can you point me to upstage right?”\n\n### **Scenario 2**\n\nYou are asked to coil cable, but you do not know whether the department wants it over-under, figure-eight, or left in place.\n\nPoor response:\n Start coiling it however you think is right.\n\nBetter response:\n “How do you want this cable coiled, or should I wait for the department?”\n\n### **Scenario 3**\n\nYou are asked to move a lift.\n\nPoor response:\n Try to figure it out.\n\nBetter response:\n “I am not trained or authorized to operate that lift.”\n\n### **Scenario 4**\n\nYou are not sure whether a case should be opened.\n\nPoor response:\n Open it and look inside.\n\nBetter response:\n “Do you want this case opened or left closed?”\n\n### **Scenario 5**\n\nYou hear a word you do not understand during instruction.\n\nPoor response:\n Pretend you know what it means.\n\nBetter response:\n “I do not know that term yet. Can you explain what you need me to do?”\n\n## **Important Safety Note**\n\nThis lesson is safety-awareness training for entry-level stagehands.
