It does not replace employer-provided safety training, site-specific instruction, union training, equipment certification, OSHA 10/30 training, or legal advice.\n\nOSHA says workers have the right to safety and health training in a language they understand, and employers are responsible for providing safety training in language and vocabulary workers can understand.\n\nFor this course, the beginner rule is simple:\n\nNever pretend you know something that affects safety, gear, people, or the flow of the job. Stop and ask.\n\n## **Key Concept**\n\nPretending is dangerous. Honesty keeps people safer, protects gear, and helps the crew work correctly. A new stagehand should never fake understanding, training, authorization, or confidence.\n\n## **Reflection Question**\n\nBefore moving on, ask yourself:\n\nWould I rather ask one question now, or guess and risk creating a bigger problem for the crew?\n\n## **Quick Knowledge Check**\n\n**Question:** What should a new stagehand do if they are asked to perform a task they do not understand or have not been trained to do?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n They should stop, communicate clearly, and ask for direction. A good response is: “I have not been trained on that,” or “Can you show me the safe way to do it?”\n\n## **Key Takeaway**\n\nNew stagehands are not expected to know everything. They are expected to be honest, safe, coachable, and willing to ask questions. Pretending to know something can cause injuries, damage gear, and create confusion. The professional choice is to ask, learn, and do the task correctly.\n\nLesson 2.7\n\n# **Module 2, Lesson 7: How to Listen During a Safety Briefing**\n\n## **Lesson Purpose**\n\nThis lesson teaches new stagehands how to pay attention during a safety briefing and use that information once the work begins.\n\nThe goal is to help learners understand that a safety briefing is not just a formality before the call starts. It may contain important information about hazards, restricted areas, emergency procedures, PPE, crew assignments, moving equipment, weather conditions, overhead work, and the safest way to move through the jobsite.\n\nA professional stagehand listens during the safety briefing because that information can prevent injuries, confusion, delays, and unsafe choices later in the call.\n\n## **Learning Objective**\n\nBy the end of this lesson, the learner should be able to explain why safety briefings matter, identify the type of information commonly shared during a briefing, and describe how to listen, ask questions, and apply the briefing during a live event call.\n\n## **Main Teaching Points**\n\nA safety briefing is a short meeting or announcement that gives workers important information before work begins or before a specific phase of work starts.\n\nIn live events, a safety briefing may happen before load-in, before load-out, before outdoor work, before a high-risk task, or when site conditions change.\n\nA safety briefing may include:\n\n● Who the leads are\n ● Where workers should report\n ● Required PPE\n ● Emergency exits and evacuation routes\n ● First aid location\n ● Restricted areas\n ● Forklift or vehicle traffic\n ● Overhead work or rigging zones\n ● Weather concerns\n ● Load-in or load-out flow\n ● Cable paths, ramps, docks, and travel routes\n ● Hazards unique to that venue or event\n ● How to report injuries, hazards, incidents, or near misses\n\nOSHA’s safety-management guidance says workers should receive information about workplace hazards and controls, know whom to contact with questions or concerns, understand how to report hazards and near misses, and know what to do in an emergency.\n\nA safety briefing tells you important site information, but it does not authorize you to perform specialized work you have not been trained and assigned to do.\n\nFor a new stagehand, the safety briefing is one of the most important moments of the call.\n\nThat is when you learn how not to become the problem.\n\n## **Short Lesson Script**\n\nBefore the crew starts working, there may be a safety briefing.\n\nSometimes it is formal. Everyone gathers together, and a lead, supervisor, safety person, venue representative, or production manager talks through the jobsite expectations.\n\nSometimes it is shorter and more informal. A lead may gather a small group and say, “Here is what we are doing, here is where we are going, here is what to watch for.”\n\nEither way, you need to listen.\n\nA safety briefing is not background noise. It is not the time to check your phone, talk to another crew member, adjust your bag, or look around like you are bored.\n\nThis is where you may learn the things that keep you safe.\n\nYou may learn that forklifts are moving through one side of the dock.\n You may learn that riggers are working overhead and certain areas are restricted.\n You may learn that the floor is wet near one entrance.\n You may learn that a ramp is only for empty cases.\n You may learn where emergency exits are.\n You may learn who your lead is.\n You may learn what PPE is required.\n You may learn where first aid is located.\n You may learn what weather conditions the crew is watching.\n You may learn which areas are off limits.\n\nIf you miss that information, you may create problems without meaning to.\n\nA new stagehand who does not listen during the briefing might walk through the wrong path, block a fire lane, push a case into a restricted area, miss a PPE requirement, or ask questions that were already answered five minutes earlier.\n\nListening well is part of being valuable.\n\nDuring the safety briefing, stand where you can hear. Face the person speaking. Put your phone away. Stop side conversations. Pay attention to names, locations, hazards, instructions, and who to ask if you are unsure.\n\nIf something is unclear, ask at the right time.\n\nYou do not need to ask questions just to look involved, but you should ask if the information affects your safety or your assignment.\n\nGood questions sound like this:\n\n“Who is my lead?”\n “Which path should we use for cases?”\n “Are there any areas we should stay out of?”\n “Where is first aid?”\n “Are forklifts using this dock path?”\n “Is overhead work still happening?”\n “What PPE is required for this area?”\n “Who should we report hazards to?”\n\nA safety briefing only helps if you use the information after it is given.\n\nIf the briefing says not to use a certain path, do not use it.\n If the briefing says hard hats are required in an area, wear one.\n If the briefing says rigging work is overhead, stay clear.\n If the briefing says report hazards to your lead, do that.\n If the briefing identifies emergency exits, do not block them.\n\nThe best stagehands do not just hear the briefing. They apply it.\n\nBut remember this: a safety briefing is not the same as specialized training.\n\nIf the briefing mentions forklifts, that does not mean you are trained to operate a forklift.\n If the briefing mentions rigging, that does not mean you are trained to rig.\n If the briefing mentions lifts, power, stage machinery, or fall protection, that does not mean you are authorized to perform that work.\n\nThe briefing helps you understand the site. It does not give you permission to do specialized tasks outside your training.\n\n## **What a Safety Briefing May Cover**\n\n| **Briefing Topic** | **Why It Matters** |\n| --- | --- |\n| Crew leads and reporting structure | Helps you know who gives direction |\n| Required PPE | Helps protect you from site-specific hazards |\n| Emergency exits | Helps keep escape routes clear and accessible |\n| First aid location | Helps workers respond quickly if someone is hurt |\n| Restricted areas | Keeps unassigned workers out of dangerous or sensitive zones |\n| Forklift and vehicle traffic | Helps prevent struck-by or crush hazards |\n| Overhead work | Helps workers avoid rigging zones and suspended loads |\n| Weather concerns | Helps outdoor crews respond to heat, wind, rain, lightning, or changing conditions |\n| Dock, ramp, and case paths | Helps gear move safely and efficiently |\n| Incident reporting process | Helps workers know how to report injuries, hazards, and near misses |\n\n## **How to Listen Like a Professional**\n\n### **1. Stop What You Are Doing**\n\nWhen the briefing starts, stop side work unless told otherwise.\n\nDo not keep organizing your bag, checking your phone, talking, or moving around.\n\n### **2. Stand Where You Can Hear**\n\nIf you cannot hear the speaker, move closer if it is safe and appropriate.\n\nDo not pretend you heard the instructions if you did not.\n\n### **3. Put Your Phone Away**\n\nUnless you were specifically told to use your phone for work information, keep it away during the briefing.\n\nA worker staring at their phone during a safety briefing looks unprepared and careless.\n\n### **4. Listen for Names**\n\nPay attention to:\n\n● Your lead\n ● Department heads\n ● Safety contact\n ● First aid contact\n ● Venue contact\n ● Truck lead\n ● Dock lead\n ● Anyone giving direct instructions to your group\n\nKnowing who to ask prevents confusion later.\n\n### **5. Listen for Locations**\n\nPay attention to:\n\n● Stage left and stage right\n ● Dock\n ● Truck area\n ● First aid location\n ● Emergency exits\n ● Restricted areas\n ● Break area\n ● Restrooms\n ● Case paths\n ● Forklift paths\n ● Outdoor weather shelter areas\n\nA lot of beginner confusion happens because someone did not listen for locations.\n\n### **6. Listen for Hazards**\n\nPay attention to warnings about:\n\n● Forklifts\n ● Ramps\n ● Dock plates\n ● Wet floors\n ● Poor lighting\n ● Cable paths\n ● Overhead rigging\n ● Suspended loads\n ● Road case traffic\n ● Weather\n ● Temporary structures\n ● Power areas\n ● Restricted zones\n\nThese are the details that may keep you from getting hurt.\n\n### **7. Listen for PPE Requirements**\n\nPPE requirements may change depending on the venue, employer, task, or area.\n\nYou may hear instructions about:\n\n● Hard hats\n ● Gloves\n ● Safety glasses\n ● High-visibility vests\n ● Hearing protection\n ● Work boots\n ● Fall protection for trained and authorized workers\n ● Weather gear for outdoor work\n\nIf you are unsure whether PPE is required, ask before entering the area.\n\n### **8. Listen for What Not to Do**\n\nSafety briefings often include restrictions.\n\nExamples:\n\n● Do not walk under rigging.\n ● Do not enter the truck unless assigned.\n ● Do not move cable without department clearance.\n ● Do not block fire lanes or exits.\n ● Do not use a certain ramp.\n ● Do not touch department gear unless directed.\n ● Do not cross forklift paths.\n ● Do not work outside during unsafe weather conditions unless cleared by leadership.\n\nRestrictions are not suggestions. They are part of the jobsite instructions.\n\n## **What to Ask During or After a Safety Briefing**\n\nA good safety question is clear, specific, and useful.\n\nGood questions include:\n\n● “Who is my direct lead?”\n ● “Where should new hands report after this?”\n ● “Which path should we use to move cases?”\n ● “Are there any areas we should avoid?”\n ● “Is overhead work happening right now?”\n ● “Are forklifts using the same path as crew?”\n ● “Where is first aid located?”\n ● “What PPE is required for this area?”\n ● “Who should we report hazards or injuries to?”\n ● “If weather changes, where should we go?”\n\nAvoid asking questions that are really complaints, jokes, or attempts to show off. Keep safety questions professional.\n\n## **What Not to Do During a Safety Briefing**\n\nA new stagehand should avoid:\n\n● Talking while the lead is speaking\n ● Looking at their phone\n ● Wandering away\n ● Standing where they cannot hear\n ● Interrupting with unrelated questions\n ● Making jokes during serious instructions\n ● Assuming the briefing does not apply to them\n ● Ignoring PPE instructions\n ● Forgetting who their lead is\n ● Asking questions later that were already answered because they were not listening\n\nThese behaviors can make a new worker look careless before the work even starts.\n\n## **Beginner Safety Briefing Checklist**\n\nDuring the briefing, try to answer these questions in your head:\n\n● Who is my lead?\n ● Where should I report?\n ● What are we doing first?\n ● What PPE is required?\n ● Where are the emergency exits?\n ● Where is first aid?\n ● What areas are restricted?\n ● Are forklifts, trucks, carts, or pallet jacks moving nearby?\n ● Is anyone working overhead?\n ● What paths should cases or gear use?\n ● What hazards did they mention?\n ● Who do I tell if I see a hazard or near miss?\n\nIf you cannot answer one of these and it affects your assignment or safety, ask.\n\n## **Example Beginner Situations**\n\n### **Situation 1: You Missed the Lead’s Name**\n\nThe safety briefing ends, and you do not know who your direct lead is.\n\nBest action: Ask before starting work.\n\nBetter response:\n “I missed who I should report to. Who is my lead for this call?”\n\n### **Situation 2: You Cannot Hear the Briefing**\n\nThe dock is loud, and you cannot hear the person speaking.\n\nBest action: Move closer if it is safe, or ask for clarification after the briefing.\n\nBetter response:\n “I could not hear the section about case paths. Which route should we use?”\n\n### **Situation 3: You Hear That Forklifts Are Active**\n\nThe briefing says forklifts will be moving near the dock.\n\nBest action: Stay alert, use approved walking routes, avoid crossing equipment paths without clearance, and never assume the operator sees you.\n\nBetter response if unsure:\n “Is this path clear for crew, or should we wait?”\n\n### **Situation 4: The Briefing Mentions Overhead Work**\n\nYou hear that riggers are working overhead during the first part of load-in.\n\nBest action: Stay out of rigging zones and avoid walking under suspended loads or overhead work.\n\nBetter response if unsure:\n “Is this area open yet, or is overhead work still active?”\n\n### **Situation 5: The Briefing Gives PPE Rules**\n\nThe briefing says hard hats and high-vis vests are required in a specific area.\n\nBest action: Wear the required PPE before entering that area.\n\nBetter response if unsure:\n “Do we need hard hats before going into the dock area?”\n\n### **Situation 6: Weather Changes After the Briefing**\n\nThe briefing mentioned possible wind at an outdoor show. Later, conditions start changing.\n\nBest action: Report the change to a lead and follow site procedures.\n\nBetter response:\n “The wind is picking up near the stage entrance. Who should we report that to?”\n\n### **Situation 7: The Briefing Mentions Equipment You Are Not Trained On**\n\nThe briefing mentions lifts, forklifts, rigging, or power areas.\n\nBest action: Use the information to avoid hazards, but do not assume you are authorized to operate or work on specialized systems.\n\nBetter response if assigned incorrectly:\n “I heard the briefing, but I have not been trained or authorized to operate that equipment.”\n\n## **How to Apply the Briefing During the Call**\n\nListening is only step one. Applying the briefing is what matters.\n\nIf the briefing said a path is restricted, do not use it.\n If the briefing said to keep exits clear, do not stage cases in front of them.\n If the briefing said to report near misses, report them.\n If the briefing said forklifts are active, stay alert around equipment paths.\n If the briefing said hard hats are required, wear one.\n If the briefing said not to touch cable until it is cleared, do not touch cable.\n If the briefing said weather is being monitored, report changing conditions.\n\nA safety briefing should guide your choices throughout the call.\n\n## **Common Beginner Mistakes**\n\n### **Mistake 1: Treating the Briefing Like a Formality**\n\nSome new workers think the briefing is just something management has to do.\n\nBetter choice: Treat the briefing as jobsite information you are expected to use.\n\n### **Mistake 2: Listening Only for Your Task**\n\nYour task matters, but hazards, exits, PPE, and emergency information matter too.\n\nBetter choice: Listen for the full picture.\n\n### **Mistake 3: Not Asking When You Miss Something**\n\nPretending you heard the instruction can create problems later.\n\nBetter choice: Ask for clarification.\n\n### **Mistake 4: Forgetting the Briefing Once Work Starts**\n\nThe briefing is not only for the first five minutes. It applies throughout the call.\n\nBetter choice: Use the briefing to guide your movement, communication, and decisions.\n\n### **Mistake 5: Assuming Every Site Is the Same**\n\nEvery venue and event may have different rules.\n\nBetter choice: Listen every time, even if you have worked similar calls before.\n\n### **Mistake 6: Confusing a Briefing With Authorization**\n\nA briefing may mention hazards, areas, and equipment, but that does not mean you are trained or authorized to perform specialized work.\n\nBetter choice: If you are not trained, authorized, assigned, and sure how to do the task safely, pause before acting and ask the correct lead.\n\n## **Practical Activity: Safety Briefing Recall**\n\nAfter reading the sample briefing below, identify the important safety information.\n\n### **Sample Briefing**\n\n“Today’s load-in will start at the dock and move toward the stage. Forklifts are active on the north side of the dock, so crew should stay on the marked walking path. Hard hats are required in the stage area until overhead work is complete. Do not walk under truss or motors. Cases should travel through the center aisle only. Keep all emergency exits and fire lanes clear. First aid is located near the production office. If you see a hazard, injury, or near miss, report it to your lead immediately.”\n\n### **Questions**\n\n- Where are forklifts active?\n\n- What PPE is required in the stage area?\n\n- What area should workers avoid?\n\n- Where should cases travel?\n\n- What paths must stay clear?\n\n- Where is first aid?\n\n- Who should hazards, injuries, or near misses be reported to?\n\n### **Best Answers**\n\n- Forklifts are active on the north side of the dock.\n\n- Hard hats are required in the stage area until overhead work is complete.\n\n- Workers should avoid walking under truss or motors.\n\n- Cases should travel through the center aisle only.\n\n- Emergency exits and fire lanes must stay clear.\n\n- First aid is near the production office.\n\n- Hazards, injuries, and near misses should be reported to the lead.\n\n## **Important Safety Note**\n\nThis lesson is safety-awareness training for entry-level stagehands. It does not replace employer-provided safety training, site-specific instruction, union training, equipment certification, OSHA 10/30 training, or legal advice.\n\nOSHA states that Outreach training is basic safety and health information and education, does not fulfill employer training requirements under OSHA standards, and is not considered certification.\n\nA safety briefing tells you important site information, but it does not authorize you to perform specialized work you have not been trained and assigned to do.\n\nFor this course, the beginner rule is simple:\n\nListen carefully during every safety briefing. If you do not understand something that affects your safety, your task, or the jobsite, ask before acting.\n\n## **Key Concept**\n\nA safety briefing gives workers important information about the jobsite before the work begins. A professional stagehand listens for hazards, PPE requirements, restricted areas, emergency information, reporting procedures, and who to ask for direction.\n\n## **Reflection Question**\n\nBefore moving on, ask yourself:\n\nIf a safety briefing ended right now, would I be able to explain who my lead is, what hazards were mentioned, what PPE is required, and where I should report a safety concern?\n\n## **Quick Knowledge Check**\n\n**Question:** What should a new stagehand do if they miss part of a safety briefing or do not understand an instruction?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n They should ask for clarification before starting work. It is better to ask a clear question than to guess and create a safety issue.\n\n## **Key Takeaway**\n\nSafety briefings are not just announcements. They tell stagehands how to work safely on that specific call. New stagehands should listen carefully, put distractions away, remember key details, ask questions when needed, and apply the briefing throughout the job. A safety briefing does not replace task-specific training or authorization. A worker who listens during the briefing is safer, more useful, and easier for leads to trust.\n\nEnd of Module 2 Quiz\n\n# **Module 2 Quiz: Safety Mindset Before Skillset**\n\n## **Quiz Instructions**\n\nChoose the best answer for each question. This quiz checks your understanding of safety awareness, worker responsibilities, jobsite hazards, near misses, safety briefings, and when to pause and ask a lead before continuing.\n\n**Recommended passing score:** 80%\n **Total questions:** 25 multiple choice questions\n **Scenario questions:** 5 written-response or discussion prompts\n\n# **Multiple Choice**\n\n## **1. Why should safety come before speed on a live event jobsite?**\n\nA. Because stagehands should work as slowly as possible\n B. Because rushing can cause injuries, damage gear, create confusion, and slow the crew down\n C. Because only supervisors are allowed to move quickly\n D. Because safety only matters during load-out\n\n**Correct Answer: B**\n\n## **2. What does it mean to “move efficiently, not recklessly”?**\n\nA. Move fast without asking questions\n B. Move with control, awareness, and clear direction\n C. Wait for someone else to do every task\n D. Ignore hazards if the schedule is tight\n\n**Correct Answer: B**\n\n## **3. Which action is the safest choice when pushing a road case through a busy work area?**\n\nA. Push as fast as possible so you finish quickly\n B. Look ahead, keep control, communicate, and watch for people or obstacles\n C. Assume everyone will move out of your way\n D. Push from behind without knowing where the case is going\n\n**Correct Answer: B**\n\n## **4. What should a new stagehand do if they are unsure whether a task is safe?**\n\nA. Guess and move quickly\n B. Ask another brand-new stagehand\n C. Pause their own work, move to a safe position if needed, and ask the correct lead\n D. Walk away without telling anyone\n\n**Correct Answer: C**\n\n## **5. What is the safest beginner rule when a task is outside your training?**\n\nA. Try it once and learn by doing\n B. Watch someone else and copy them\n C. Say you have not been trained and ask for proper direction\n D. Do it quickly before anyone notices\n\n**Correct Answer: C**\n\n## **6. Which statement best describes safety responsibility on a jobsite?**\n\nA. Safety is only the employer’s responsibility\n B. Safety is only the worker’s responsibility\n C. Safety is a shared responsibility between employers, leads, and workers\n D. Safety only matters when someone gets hurt\n\n**Correct Answer: C**\n\n## **7. Which of the following is a general worker safety right?**\n\nA. The right to ignore PPE rules\n B. The right to a safe workplace and safety information workers can understand\n C. The right to operate any equipment on-site\n D. The right to move gear without direction\n\n**Correct Answer: B**\n\n## **8. Which of the following is a worker responsibility?**\n\nA. Follow safety rules and report hazards\n B. Create your own work plan without asking\n C. Enter restricted areas to look busy\n D. Move department gear without permission\n\n**Correct Answer: A**\n\n## **9. What should a new stagehand do if they see a road case blocking an emergency exit?**\n\nA. Ignore it unless someone complains\n B. Sit on the case so people notice it\n C. Report it to the correct lead immediately\n D. Push it anywhere without asking\n\n**Correct Answer: C**\n\n## **10. What is a hazard?**\n\nA. Something that could cause harm\n B. A person who asks too many questions\n C. A task that takes too long\n D. A rule that only applies to supervisors\n\n**Correct Answer: A**\n\n## **11. What is risk?**\n\nA. The chance that a hazard could cause harm and how serious the outcome could be\n B. Any piece of gear with wheels\n C. A worker who is new\n D. The same thing as PPE\n\n**Correct Answer: A**\n\n## **12. What is unsafe behavior?**\n\nA. Asking a lead for clarification\n B. An action that increases danger\n C. Waiting for a forklift path to clear\n D. Reporting a wet floor\n\n**Correct Answer: B**\n\n## **13. Which example shows the difference between a hazard and a risk?**\n\nA. A cable is the hazard; someone tripping over it is the risk\n B. A lead is the hazard; a stagehand is the risk\n C. A hard hat is the hazard; PPE is the risk\n D. A break is the hazard; lunch is the risk\n\n**Correct Answer: A**\n\n## **14. Which condition can increase the risk of a cable path?**\n\nA. Good lighting and clear markings\n B. Low light, tired workers, and cases moving through the area\n C. A clear walking path\n D. A lead standing nearby\n\n**Correct Answer: B**\n\n## **15. What should a new stagehand do if they notice an unstable stack of road cases?**\n\nA. Climb on the cases to test them\n B. Push the stack to see if it moves\n C. Stay clear and report it to the correct lead\n D. Ignore it unless it falls\n\n**Correct Answer: C**\n\n## **16. What does a “pause and get a lead” mindset mean?**\n\nA. Refuse every task\n B. Stop the entire show whenever you are nervous\n C. Pause your own action when something is unclear, unsafe, or outside your training\n D. Leave the work area without telling anyone\n\n**Correct Answer: C**\n\n## **17. Which situation should cause a new stagehand to pause and ask for direction?**\n\nA. They clearly understand the task and the path is safe\n B. They are asked to pull a cable but do not know whether it is still connected\n C. Their lead tells them where to stand\n D. They are waiting for the next instruction\n\n**Correct Answer: B**\n\n## **18. What should a new stagehand say if asked to operate a lift they have not been trained or authorized to use?**\n\nA. “I’ll try it carefully.”\n B. “I have not been trained or authorized on that equipment.”\n C. “I watched someone do it once.”\n D. “I can probably figure it out.”\n\n**Correct Answer: B**\n\n## **19. What is a near miss?**\n\nA. A task that was completed early\n B. An event where someone could have been hurt, but was not\n C. A stagehand who arrives late\n D. A piece of gear that belongs to another department\n\n**Correct Answer: B**\n\n## **20. Why should near misses be reported?**\n\nA. To blame someone\n B. To slow down the crew\n C. To help identify and correct hazards before someone gets hurt\n D. To avoid telling the lead what happened\n\n**Correct Answer: C**\n\n## **21. What should happen first if someone is seriously hurt or there is immediate danger?**\n\nA. Fill out a report before doing anything else\n B. Follow the site emergency procedure and get help\n C. Keep working until break\n D. Post about it online\n\n**Correct Answer: B**\n\n## **22. Which of the following should be reported?**\n\nA. An injury\n B. Gear damage\n C. A near miss\n D. All of the above\n\n**Correct Answer: D**\n\n## **23. Why should a new stagehand never pretend to know something?**\n\nA. Because pretending can lead to unsafe actions, damaged gear, and confusion\n B. Because leads do not like questions\n C. Because beginners are not allowed to learn\n D. Because all tasks are too dangerous\n\n**Correct Answer: A**\n\n## **24. What should a new stagehand do during a safety briefing?**\n\nA. Check their phone\n B. Talk to another crew member\n C. Listen for hazards, PPE rules, restricted areas, emergency information, and who their lead is\n D. Walk away if they have worked at the venue before\n\n**Correct Answer: C**\n\n## **25. What does a safety briefing not do?**\n\nA. Provide useful jobsite information\n B. Explain hazards and procedures\n C. Tell workers who to report to\n D. Authorize a worker to perform specialized work they have not been trained and assigned to do\n\n**Correct Answer: D**\n\n# **Scenario Questions**\n\nThese can be used as written-response questions, discussion prompts, or instructor-reviewed answers.\n\n## **Scenario 1: The Rushed Case Push**\n\nYou are working load-out. The crew is moving quickly, and someone tells you to push a road case across a dark area near the dock. You cannot clearly see the path, and you know forklifts have been active nearby.\n\n**Question:** What should you do?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n Pause your own work and do not push blindly into the dark path. Move to a safe position if needed, communicate the concern, and ask the correct lead whether the path is clear or whether another route should be used. Speed should never replace safety.\n\n## **Scenario 2: The Unknown Cable**\n\nA crew member says, “Grab that cable and pull it back.” You are not sure what department it belongs to or whether it is still connected.\n\n**Question:** What should you do?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n Do not pull the cable until it has been cleared by the correct department or lead. A good response would be, “Has this cable been cleared to pull?” or “Should we check with the department before moving it?”\n\n## **Scenario 3: The Near Miss**\n\nA road case starts rolling down a slight ramp and almost hits a worker, but someone stops it in time. Nobody is hurt.\n\n**Question:** Should this be reported? Why or why not?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n Yes. This is a near miss. Even though nobody was hurt, the situation showed that someone could have been injured. It should be reported to the correct lead so the crew can check the ramp, case brakes, parking location, or work process before it happens again.\n\n## **Scenario 4: The Safety Briefing**\n\nDuring the safety briefing, the lead says hard hats are required near the stage until overhead work is complete. Later, you see another new stagehand walking into that area without a hard hat.\n\n**Question:** What is the professional response?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n Do not ignore it or create conflict. Calmly warn the worker if appropriate and report the issue to the correct lead. The briefing information should be applied during the call, and PPE requirements should be followed.\n\n## **Scenario 5: The Untrained Equipment Request**\n\nSomeone asks you to move an aerial lift “just a few feet.” You have never been trained or authorized to operate it.\n\n**Question:** What should you say or do?\n\n**Best Answer:**\n Do not operate the lift. Say clearly, “I have not been trained or authorized to operate that equipment. We need someone qualified.” Then follow direction from the correct lead.\n\n# **Answer Key**\n\n- B\n\n- B\n\n- B\n\n- C\n\n- C\n\n- C\n\n- B\n\n- A\n\n- C\n\n- A\n\n- A\n\n- B\n\n- A\n\n- B\n\n- C\n\n- C\n\n- B\n\n- B\n\n- B\n\n- C\n\n- B\n\n- D\n\n- A\n\n- C\n\n- D\n\nModule 3\n\nTab 26\n\nTab 27\n\nTab 28\n\nTab 29\n\nTab 30\n\nTab 31\n\nTab 32\n\nModule 4\n\nTab 33\n\nTab 34\n\nTab 35\n\nTab 36\n\nTab 37\n\nTab 38\n\nTab 39\n\nTab 40\n\nModule 5\n\nTab 41\n\nTab 42\n\nTab 43\n\nTab 44\n\nTab 45\n\nTab 46\n\nTab 47\n\nTab 48\n\nTab 49\n\nTab 50\n\nModule 6\n\nTab 51\n\nTab 52\n\nTab 53\n\nTab 54\n\nTab 55\n\nTab 56\n\nTab 57\n\nTab 58\n\nTab 59\n\nTab 60\n\nModule 7\n\nTab 61\n\nTab 62\n\nTab 63\n\nTab 64\n\nTab 65\n\nTab 66\n\nTab 67\n\nTab 68\n\nTab 69\n\nModule 8\n\nTab 70\n\nTab 71\n\nTab 72\n\nTab 73\n\nTab 74\n\nTab 75\n\nTab 76\n\nTab 77\n\nTab 78\n\nModule 9\n\nTab 79\n\nTab 80\n\nTab 81\n\nTab 82\n\nTab 83\n\nTab 84\n\nTab 85\n\nTab 86\n\nTab 87\n\nModule 10\n\nTab 88\n\nTab 89\n\nTab 90\n\nTab 91\n\nTab 92\n\nTab 93\n\nTab 94\n\nTab 95\n\nTab 96","stderr":""}Good — I have everything. New Stagehand Valuable<\/h3>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:buttons -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-buttons\"><div class=\"wp-block-button is-style-outline\"><a class=\"wp-block-button__link wp-element-button\" href=\"\/module-1-welcome-to-the-live-event-world\/lesson-1-5-what-makes-a-new-stagehand-valuable\/\" style=\"border-radius:999px;color:#f6c65b;border-color:#f6c65b\">Start Lesson<\/a><\/div><\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:buttons -->\n<\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:group -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"12px\"} --><div style=\"height:12px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" Module 1 page live at ID 320. Now building all 5 lesson pages for Module 1, then Module 2 and its 7 lessons. Starting with Lesson 1.1:{"data":{"id":321,"status":"publish","type":"page","date":"2026-05-25T14:09:42","modified":"2026-05-25T14:09:42","link":"https:\/\/thecrewblueprint.wpcomstaging.com\/general-site\/stagehand-fundamentals\/module-1-welcome-to-the-live-event-world\/lesson-1-1-what-a-stagehand-actually-does\/","author":280519757,"parent":320,"menu_order":0,"template":"","featured_media":0,"comment_status":"closed","meta":{"_crdt_document":"","advanced_seo_description":"","jetpack_seo_html_title":"","jetpack_seo_noindex":false,"footnotes":"","_yoast_wpseo_title":"","_yoast_wpseo_metadesc":"","_yoast_wpseo_focuskw":"","rank_math_title":"","rank_math_description":"","rank_math_focus_keyword":"","_aioseop_title":"","_aioseop_description":""},"title":"Lesson 1.1: What a Stagehand Actually Does","content":"<!-- wp:group {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"background\":\"#0b0d10\"}},\"className\":\"alignfull\",\"layout\":{\"type\":\"constrained\"}} -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-group alignfull has-background\" style=\"background-color:#0b0d10;padding-top:60px;padding-bottom:80px;padding-left:24px;padding-right:24px\">\n\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f6c65b\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"13px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\",\"letterSpacing\":\"0.12em\",\"textTransform\":\"uppercase\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#f6c65b;font-size:13px;font-weight:800;letter-spacing:0.12em;text-transform:uppercase\">Module 1 &mdash; Lesson 1.1<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":1,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"44px\",\"fontWeight\":\"900\",\"letterSpacing\":\"-0.04em\"}}} -->\n<h1 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:44px;font-weight:900;letter-spacing:-0.04em\">What a Stagehand Actually Does<\/h1>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"14px\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;font-size:14px\"><a href=\"\/general-site\/stagehand-fundamentals\/\" style=\"color:#f6c65b\">&larr; Course Home<\/a> &nbsp;&bull;&nbsp; <a href=\"\/general-site\/stagehand-fundamentals\/module-1-welcome-to-the-live-event-world\/\" style=\"color:#f6c65b\">&larr; Module 1<\/a><\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"32px\"} --><div style=\"height:32px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:group {\"style\":{\"border\":{\"color\":\"#303846\",\"width\":\"1px\",\"radius\":\"20px\"},\"color\":{\"background\":\"#141820\"},\"spacing\":{\"padding\":{\"top\":\"40px\",\"bottom\":\"40px\",\"left\":\"40px\",\"right\":\"40px\"}}},\"layout\":{\"type\":\"constrained\"}} -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-group has-border-color has-background\" style=\"border-color:#303846;border-width:1px;border-radius:20px;background-color:#141820;padding:40px\">\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"26px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:26px;font-weight:800\">What You Will Learn<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n\n<!-- wp:list {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"}}} -->\n<ul style=\"color:#c6c8cc\"><li>What a stagehand is and what the job actually involves<\/li><li>Why the role is more skilled and more physical than most people expect<\/li><li>What the word &ldquo;call&rdquo; means in live event work<\/li><li>Why stagehands are the foundation of every live event<\/li><\/ul>\n<!-- \/wp:list -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"24px\"} --><div style=\"height:24px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"26px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:26px;font-weight:800\">The Short Answer<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">A stagehand is a production worker who builds, moves, operates, and tears down the physical and technical elements that make live events happen. If there is a stage, a speaker system, a lighting rig, a video wall, a set piece, or a truss hanging in the air, a stagehand helped put it there.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">Stagehands work concerts, corporate events, trade shows, award ceremonies, theater productions, sporting events, festivals, and more. The work is physical, technical, and fast-moving. It requires attention to detail, the ability to follow direction, and a willingness to work hard in environments that are often loud, crowded, and deadline-driven.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"24px\"} --><div style=\"height:24px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"26px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:26px;font-weight:800\">What the Job Actually Looks Like<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">On any given day, a stagehand might be unloading trucks, running cable, assembling truss, hanging speakers, building a stage deck, setting up drape, pushing road cases, connecting power, or striking everything back down at the end of the night. The specific tasks depend on the type of event, the department you are assigned to, and what the production needs that day.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">You will rarely know every detail of the job before you show up. That is normal. Part of being a stagehand is being ready to learn quickly, take direction well, and work effectively with people you may have just met.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"24px\"} --><div style=\"height:24px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"26px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:26px;font-weight:800\">Understanding the Word &ldquo;Call&rdquo;<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">In the live event industry, a work assignment is called a <strong style=\"color:#f7f7f2\">call<\/strong>. When you are booked for a job, you are &ldquo;on a call.&rdquo; The call has a call time, which is when you are expected to report to the venue, ready to work. Being on time to a call is one of the most important professional habits you can develop. In this industry, late is unacceptable.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"24px\"} --><div style=\"height:24px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"26px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:26px;font-weight:800\">Why Stagehands Matter<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">Without stagehands, nothing gets built. The artist, the production manager, the lighting designer, the audio engineer &mdash; none of them can do their jobs if the physical infrastructure is not in place. Stagehands are the people who make the environment possible. That is not a small thing. That is everything.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">The best stagehands are not just laborers. They are problem-solvers, communicators, and professionals who take ownership of their work. That is what this course is designed to help you become.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"32px\"} --><div style=\"height:32px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:group {\"style\":{\"border\":{\"color\":\"#f6c65b\",\"width\":\"1px\",\"radius\":\"12px\"},\"color\":{\"background\":\"#1a1600\"},\"spacing\":{\"padding\":{\"top\":\"20px\",\"bottom\":\"20px\",\"left\":\"24px\",\"right\":\"24px\"}}},\"layout\":{\"type\":\"constrained\"}} -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-group has-border-color has-background\" style=\"border-color:#f6c65b;border-width:1px;border-radius:12px;background-color:#1a1600;padding:20px 24px\">\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f6c65b\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"12px\",\"fontWeight\":\"900\",\"letterSpacing\":\"0.1em\",\"textTransform\":\"uppercase\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#f6c65b;font-size:12px;font-weight:900;letter-spacing:0.1em;text-transform:uppercase\">Key Takeaway<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"16px\",\"lineHeight\":\"1.65\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:16px;line-height:1.65\">A stagehand builds, moves, operates, and tears down the physical and technical elements of live events. The work is skilled, physical, and essential. Without stagehands, the show does not happen.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:group -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"40px\"} --><div style=\"height:40px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:buttons -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-buttons\"><div class=\"wp-block-button\"><a class=\"wp-block-button__link wp-element-button\" href=\"\/general-site\/stagehand-fundamentals\/module-1-welcome-to-the-live-event-world\/lesson-1-2-load-in-show-call-load-out\/\" style=\"border-radius:999px;background-color:#f6c65b;color:#101010;font-weight:800\">Next Lesson: Load-In, Show Call, and Load-Out &rarr;<\/a><\/div><\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:buttons -->\n\n<\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:group -->\n\n<\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:group -->","excerpt":""}}Lesson 1.1 live ✓. Now Lesson 1.2:{"data":{"id":322,"status":"publish","type":"page","date":"2026-05-25T14:10:44","modified":"2026-05-25T14:10:44","link":"https:\/\/thecrewblueprint.wpcomstaging.com\/general-site\/stagehand-fundamentals\/module-1-welcome-to-the-live-event-world\/lesson-1-2-load-in-show-call-load-out\/","author":280519757,"parent":320,"menu_order":0,"template":"","featured_media":0,"comment_status":"closed","meta":{"_crdt_document":"","advanced_seo_description":"","jetpack_seo_html_title":"","jetpack_seo_noindex":false,"footnotes":"","_yoast_wpseo_title":"","_yoast_wpseo_metadesc":"","_yoast_wpseo_focuskw":"","rank_math_title":"","rank_math_description":"","rank_math_focus_keyword":"","_aioseop_title":"","_aioseop_description":""},"title":"Lesson 1.2: The Difference Between Load-In, Show Call, and Load-Out","content":"<!-- wp:group {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"background\":\"#0b0d10\"}},\"className\":\"alignfull\",\"layout\":{\"type\":\"constrained\"}} -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-group alignfull has-background\" style=\"background-color:#0b0d10;padding-top:60px;padding-bottom:80px;padding-left:24px;padding-right:24px\">\n\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f6c65b\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"13px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\",\"letterSpacing\":\"0.12em\",\"textTransform\":\"uppercase\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#f6c65b;font-size:13px;font-weight:800;letter-spacing:0.12em;text-transform:uppercase\">Module 1 &mdash; Lesson 1.2<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":1,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"44px\",\"fontWeight\":\"900\",\"letterSpacing\":\"-0.04em\"}}} -->\n<h1 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:44px;font-weight:900;letter-spacing:-0.04em\">The Difference Between Load-In, Show Call, and Load-Out<\/h1>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"14px\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;font-size:14px\"><a href=\"\/general-site\/stagehand-fundamentals\/\" style=\"color:#f6c65b\">&larr; Course Home<\/a> &nbsp;&bull;&nbsp; <a href=\"\/general-site\/stagehand-fundamentals\/module-1-welcome-to-the-live-event-world\/\" style=\"color:#f6c65b\">&larr; Module 1<\/a><\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"32px\"} --><div style=\"height:32px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:group {\"style\":{\"border\":{\"color\":\"#303846\",\"width\":\"1px\",\"radius\":\"20px\"},\"color\":{\"background\":\"#141820\"},\"spacing\":{\"padding\":{\"top\":\"40px\",\"bottom\":\"40px\",\"left\":\"40px\",\"right\":\"40px\"}}},\"layout\":{\"type\":\"constrained\"}} -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-group has-border-color has-background\" style=\"border-color:#303846;border-width:1px;border-radius:20px;background-color:#141820;padding:40px\">\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"26px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:26px;font-weight:800\">What You Will Learn<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:list {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"}}} -->\n<ul style=\"color:#c6c8cc\"><li>The three main phases of a live event call: load-in, show call, and load-out<\/li><li>What happens during each phase and what is expected of you<\/li><li>How the pace, intensity, and focus shift between each phase<\/li><li>Why understanding the structure of a call makes you a better crew member from day one<\/li><\/ul>\n<!-- \/wp:list -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"24px\"} --><div style=\"height:24px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"26px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:26px;font-weight:800\">The Three Phases of a Call<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">Every live event production follows a general structure. It may look different depending on the type of show, the size of the crew, or the venue, but the core phases are almost always the same: load-in, show call, and load-out. Understanding these phases is one of the first things you need to know as a new stagehand.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"20px\"} --><div style=\"height:20px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:group {\"style\":{\"border\":{\"color\":\"#f6c65b\",\"width\":\"1px\",\"radius\":\"12px\"},\"color\":{\"background\":\"#1a1600\"},\"spacing\":{\"padding\":{\"top\":\"24px\",\"bottom\":\"24px\",\"left\":\"24px\",\"right\":\"24px\"}}},\"layout\":{\"type\":\"constrained\"}} -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-group has-border-color has-background\" style=\"border-color:#f6c65b;border-width:1px;border-radius:12px;background-color:#1a1600;padding:24px\">\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f6c65b\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"12px\",\"fontWeight\":\"900\",\"letterSpacing\":\"0.1em\",\"textTransform\":\"uppercase\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#f6c65b;font-size:12px;font-weight:900;letter-spacing:0.1em;text-transform:uppercase\">Phase 1<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":3,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"22px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h3 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:22px;font-weight:800\">Load-In<\/h3>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">Load-in is the build phase. This is when the crew arrives at the venue and begins constructing the event. Trucks are unloaded, equipment is moved into position, and the technical systems &mdash; audio, lighting, video, rigging, staging &mdash; are assembled and tested.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">Load-in can last a few hours or several days, depending on the scale of the production. A small corporate event might load in over a morning. A major touring concert might require multiple days of crew work before the show is ready.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">During load-in, you will be expected to follow direction from department leads, work efficiently with your crew, and stay aware of what is happening around you. Load-in is physically demanding and often involves a lot of lifting, carrying, and moving. Pace yourself, but keep moving.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:group -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"16px\"} --><div style=\"height:16px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:group {\"style\":{\"border\":{\"color\":\"#303846\",\"width\":\"1px\",\"radius\":\"12px\"},\"color\":{\"background\":\"#1a2030\"},\"spacing\":{\"padding\":{\"top\":\"24px\",\"bottom\":\"24px\",\"left\":\"24px\",\"right\":\"24px\"}}},\"layout\":{\"type\":\"constrained\"}} -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-group has-border-color has-background\" style=\"border-color:#303846;border-width:1px;border-radius:12px;background-color:#1a2030;padding:24px\">\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f6c65b\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"12px\",\"fontWeight\":\"900\",\"letterSpacing\":\"0.1em\",\"textTransform\":\"uppercase\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#f6c65b;font-size:12px;font-weight:900;letter-spacing:0.1em;text-transform:uppercase\">Phase 2<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":3,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"22px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h3 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:22px;font-weight:800\">Show Call<\/h3>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">The show call is when the event is actually happening &mdash; the audience is in the room, the artist is on stage, or the presentation is live. During the show, crew members may be running the event, managing equipment, handling quick changes, or simply standing by in case something needs attention.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">Not all stagehands work the show call. Some are hired only for load-in and load-out. If you are on a show call, your job is to stay focused, stay quiet unless spoken to, and be ready to respond quickly if something is needed. The show is live. Mistakes are visible. Professionalism is everything.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:group -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"16px\"} --><div style=\"height:16px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:group {\"style\":{\"border\":{\"color\":\"#303846\",\"width\":\"1px\",\"radius\":\"12px\"},\"color\":{\"background\":\"#1a1820\"},\"spacing\":{\"padding\":{\"top\":\"24px\",\"bottom\":\"24px\",\"left\":\"24px\",\"right\":\"24px\"}}},\"layout\":{\"type\":\"constrained\"}} -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-group has-border-color has-background\" style=\"border-color:#303846;border-width:1px;border-radius:12px;background-color:#1a1820;padding:24px\">\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f6c65b\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"12px\",\"fontWeight\":\"900\",\"letterSpacing\":\"0.1em\",\"textTransform\":\"uppercase\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#f6c65b;font-size:12px;font-weight:900;letter-spacing:0.1em;text-transform:uppercase\">Phase 3<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":3,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"22px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h3 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:22px;font-weight:800\">Load-Out<\/h3>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">Load-out is the strike phase. Once the event is over, everything that was built gets taken down, packed up, and loaded back into the trucks. Load-out is often the most physically intense part of the day, and it frequently happens late at night or in the early hours of the morning when everyone is tired.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">Load-out requires the same level of professionalism and focus as load-in. Equipment needs to be handled properly, packed correctly, and loaded safely. Cutting corners during load-out leads to damaged gear, injured crew members, and a reputation for being sloppy. Do not be that person.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">Load-out ends when the last truck is packed and the venue is cleared. That is when your call is over.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:group -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"24px\"} --><div style=\"height:24px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"26px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:26px;font-weight:800\">One More Thing: Not Every Call Has All Three Phases<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">Some calls are load-in only. Some are load-out only. Some are all three back to back. When you get a call, pay attention to what phase you are being hired for and what your start time and expected end time are. Ask your steward or lead if you are unsure. Never assume.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"24px\"} --><div style=\"height:24px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:group {\"style\":{\"border\":{\"color\":\"#f6c65b\",\"width\":\"1px\",\"radius\":\"12px\"},\"color\":{\"background\":\"#1a1600\"},\"spacing\":{\"padding\":{\"top\":\"20px\",\"bottom\":\"20px\",\"left\":\"24px\",\"right\":\"24px\"}}},\"layout\":{\"type\":\"constrained\"}} -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-group has-border-color has-background\" style=\"border-color:#f6c65b;border-width:1px;border-radius:12px;background-color:#1a1600;padding:20px 24px\">\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f6c65b\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"12px\",\"fontWeight\":\"900\",\"letterSpacing\":\"0.1em\",\"textTransform\":\"uppercase\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#f6c65b;font-size:12px;font-weight:900;letter-spacing:0.1em;text-transform:uppercase\">Key Takeaway<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"16px\",\"lineHeight\":\"1.65\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:16px;line-height:1.65\">Every live event has three phases: load-in (build), show call (live event), and load-out (strike). Each phase has different demands. Know which phase you are working, what is expected of you, and stay professional throughout all of it.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:group -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"40px\"} --><div style=\"height:40px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:buttons -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-buttons\"><div class=\"wp-block-button\"><a class=\"wp-block-button__link wp-element-button\" href=\"\/general-site\/stagehand-fundamentals\/module-1-welcome-to-the-live-event-world\/lesson-1-3-common-work-environments\/\" style=\"border-radius:999px;background-color:#f6c65b;color:#101010;font-weight:800\">Next Lesson: Common Work Environments &rarr;<\/a><\/div><\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:buttons -->\n\n<\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:group -->\n\n<\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:group -->","excerpt":""}}Lesson 1.2 live ✓. Lessons 1.3, 1.4, and 1.5 now:{"data":{"id":323,"status":"publish","type":"page","date":"2026-05-25T14:11:35","modified":"2026-05-25T14:11:35","link":"https:\/\/thecrewblueprint.wpcomstaging.com\/general-site\/stagehand-fundamentals\/module-1-welcome-to-the-live-event-world\/lesson-1-3-common-work-environments\/","author":280519757,"parent":320,"menu_order":0,"template":"","featured_media":0,"comment_status":"closed","meta":{"_crdt_document":"","advanced_seo_description":"","jetpack_seo_html_title":"","jetpack_seo_noindex":false,"footnotes":"","_yoast_wpseo_title":"","_yoast_wpseo_metadesc":"","_yoast_wpseo_focuskw":"","rank_math_title":"","rank_math_description":"","rank_math_focus_keyword":"","_aioseop_title":"","_aioseop_description":""},"title":"Lesson 1.3: Common Work Environments","content":"<!-- wp:group {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"background\":\"#0b0d10\"}},\"className\":\"alignfull\",\"layout\":{\"type\":\"constrained\"}} -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-group alignfull has-background\" style=\"background-color:#0b0d10;padding-top:60px;padding-bottom:80px;padding-left:24px;padding-right:24px\">\n\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f6c65b\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"13px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\",\"letterSpacing\":\"0.12em\",\"textTransform\":\"uppercase\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#f6c65b;font-size:13px;font-weight:800;letter-spacing:0.12em;text-transform:uppercase\">Module 1 &mdash; Lesson 1.3<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":1,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"44px\",\"fontWeight\":\"900\",\"letterSpacing\":\"-0.04em\"}}} -->\n<h1 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:44px;font-weight:900;letter-spacing:-0.04em\">Common Work Environments<\/h1>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"14px\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;font-size:14px\"><a href=\"\/general-site\/stagehand-fundamentals\/\" style=\"color:#f6c65b\">&larr; Course Home<\/a> &nbsp;&bull;&nbsp; <a href=\"\/general-site\/stagehand-fundamentals\/module-1-welcome-to-the-live-event-world\/\" style=\"color:#f6c65b\">&larr; Module 1<\/a><\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"32px\"} --><div style=\"height:32px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:group {\"style\":{\"border\":{\"color\":\"#303846\",\"width\":\"1px\",\"radius\":\"20px\"},\"color\":{\"background\":\"#141820\"},\"spacing\":{\"padding\":{\"top\":\"40px\",\"bottom\":\"40px\",\"left\":\"40px\",\"right\":\"40px\"}}},\"layout\":{\"type\":\"constrained\"}} -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-group has-border-color has-background\" style=\"border-color:#303846;border-width:1px;border-radius:20px;background-color:#141820;padding:40px\">\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"26px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:26px;font-weight:800\">What You Will Learn<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:list {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"}}} -->\n<ul style=\"color:#c6c8cc\"><li>The most common types of venues and events where stagehands work<\/li><li>How the environment affects the pace, layout, and expectations of the job<\/li><li>Why adaptability is one of the most valuable traits a stagehand can have<\/li><\/ul>\n<!-- \/wp:list -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"24px\"} --><div style=\"height:24px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"26px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:26px;font-weight:800\">No Two Venues Are the Same<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">As a stagehand, you will work in a wide variety of locations. Each environment has its own layout, its own rules, its own loading dock, and its own crew culture. Learning to read a new space quickly and adapt to it is one of the core skills of this job.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"20px\"} --><div style=\"height:20px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"22px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:22px;font-weight:800\">Arenas and Stadiums<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">Arenas and stadiums host large-scale concerts, sporting events, award shows, and major corporate productions. These venues have large load-in docks, massive floor space, and high production ceilings. Crews are typically larger, calls can run multiple days, and the scale of the equipment is significant. Working in an arena often means operating alongside union locals and larger production departments with strict protocols.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"22px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:22px;font-weight:800\">Convention Centers and Hotels<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">Convention centers and hotels are common corporate event environments. You might be setting up general session stages, breakout rooms, trade show booths, or banquet configurations. These environments tend to be more client-facing, meaning professionalism in appearance and behavior is especially important. Crews are often smaller and work faster to meet tighter business timelines.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"22px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:22px;font-weight:800\">Theaters and Performing Arts Centers<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">Theaters have their own vocabulary, their own fly systems, and their own stage terminology. Working in a theater environment means understanding fly rails, line sets, counterweights, and the specific safety protocols of that space. Many theaters are union houses with jurisdiction rules that govern what crew members can and cannot do.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"22px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:22px;font-weight:800\">Outdoor Festivals and Amphitheaters<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">Outdoor events bring a different set of challenges: weather, uneven terrain, sun exposure, and no climate control. Festival sites may have multiple stages running simultaneously, with large crews split across different areas of a grounds. Amphitheaters combine elements of indoor and outdoor work. Hydration, sun protection, and situational awareness are especially important in these environments.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"22px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:22px;font-weight:800\">Clubs, Ballrooms, and Smaller Venues<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">Not every call is a large production. Smaller venues &mdash; clubs, ballrooms, and event spaces &mdash; often have tighter load-in areas, lower ceilings, and less room to work. Smaller shows can move quickly and require crew members who are flexible and self-sufficient. These environments are also common places where newer stagehands get early experience.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"24px\"} --><div style=\"height:24px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:heading {\"level\":2,\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"26px\",\"fontWeight\":\"800\"}}} -->\n<h2 style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:26px;font-weight:800\">The Common Thread<\/h2>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#c6c8cc\"},\"typography\":{\"lineHeight\":\"1.75\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#c6c8cc;line-height:1.75\">No matter where you work, the fundamentals stay the same: show up on time, follow direction, work safely, and take care of the equipment. The environment changes. The professionalism does not.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"24px\"} --><div style=\"height:24px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:group {\"style\":{\"border\":{\"color\":\"#f6c65b\",\"width\":\"1px\",\"radius\":\"12px\"},\"color\":{\"background\":\"#1a1600\"},\"spacing\":{\"padding\":{\"top\":\"20px\",\"bottom\":\"20px\",\"left\":\"24px\",\"right\":\"24px\"}}},\"layout\":{\"type\":\"constrained\"}} -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-group has-border-color has-background\" style=\"border-color:#f6c65b;border-width:1px;border-radius:12px;background-color:#1a1600;padding:20px 24px\">\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f6c65b\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"12px\",\"fontWeight\":\"900\",\"letterSpacing\":\"0.1em\",\"textTransform\":\"uppercase\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#f6c65b;font-size:12px;font-weight:900;letter-spacing:0.1em;text-transform:uppercase\">Key Takeaway<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph {\"style\":{\"color\":{\"text\":\"#f7f7f2\"},\"typography\":{\"fontSize\":\"16px\",\"lineHeight\":\"1.65\"}}} -->\n<p style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:16px;line-height:1.65\">Stagehands work in arenas, convention centers, theaters, outdoor festivals, and smaller venues. Each environment has its own rules and pace. Adaptability is essential. The core standards of professionalism, safety, and reliability apply everywhere.<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:group -->\n\n<!-- wp:spacer {\"height\":\"40px\"} --><div style=\"height:40px\" aria-hidden=\"true\" class=\"wp-block-spacer\"><\/div><!-- \/wp:spacer -->\n\n<!-- wp:buttons -->\n<div class=\"wp-block-buttons\"><div class=\"wp-block-button\"><a class=\"wp-block-button__link wp-element-button\" href=\"\/general-site\/stagehand-fundamentals\/module-1-welcome-to-the-live-event-world\/lesson-1-4-who-is-on-the-crew\/\" style=\"border-radius:999px;background-color:#f6c65b;color:#101010;font-weight:800\">Next Lesson: Who Is on the Crew &rarr;<\/a><\/div><\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:buttons -->\n\n<\/div>\n<!-- \/wp:group style=\"color:#f6c65b;font-size:13px;font-weight:800;letter-spacing:0.12em;text-transform:uppercase\">Module 1 - Lesson 1.4<\/p>\n<!-- \/wp:paragraph -->\n<!-- wp:heading style=\"color:#f7f7f2;font-size:44px;font-weight:900;letter-spacing:-0.04em\">Who Is on the Crew<\/h1>\n<!-- \/wp:heading -->\n<!-- wp:paragraph
